The Legal Nature of Iqalah: Faskh or Bay‘?
Commentary on the nature of iqalah and its practical consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi positions on iqalah and apply their practical consequences in five scenarios.

Core Idea
Iqalah is faskh for the parties but a sale for third parties according to Abu Hanifa; the classification determines key rulings.

Iqalah, the mutual rescission of a sale, seems straightforward, but the Hanafi school has a rich internal debate: is it a mere rescission (faskh) or a new sale (bay‘)? The answer changes how we handle returns, gifts, re-sales, and even possession of measured goods. In this lesson, we trace the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and see how the five practical issues resolve.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three Hanafi imams' core positions
Read the text to find each imam's default view: Abu Hanifa (faskh for parties, sale for third party), Abu Yusuf (sale generally), Muhammad (faskh generally). Note their exceptions.
The exceptions are triggered when making it faskh or sale is impossible.
2
Analyze the exceptions for each imam
Determine what 'impossible' means: birth/destruction after qabd, sale for more/less price, different genus, etc. Understand how each imam's exceptions flow from their default.
For Abu Yusuf, if sale is impossible before qabd, it becomes faskh; if both impossible, it is void.
3
Examine the five practical consequences (faskh for parties)
List the five issues where considering iqalah as faskh yields a distinct ruling: obligation to return only first price, no invalidation by corrupt conditions, validity of immediate resale, validity of gift after iqalah, and possession without re-measurement.
These show why the classification matters in real transactions.
4
Apply the classification to third-party scenarios
Consider the five (or more) issues where iqalah is treated as a sale regarding third parties: e.g., iqalah after pledge requires pledgee's consent, iqalah of a slave girl requires istibra'.
These extensions demonstrate the hybrid nature of iqalah.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon position)
Based on the text's structure and the author's commentary, Abu Hanifa's view is presented as the default and the five issues are built upon it, indicating it is the mufta bihi.
However, in practice, the details from all three imams may be considered depending on the case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (هي فسخ في حق المتعاقدين بيع في حق ثالث وهذا عند أبي حنيفة إلا إن تعذر جعلها فسخاً بأن ولدت المبيعة بعد القبض أو هلك المبيع فإنها تبطل ويبقى البيع على حاله. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان قبل القبض أو بعده، وروي عن أبي حنيفة أنها فسخ قبل في البدائع. وظاهره ترجيح الإطلاق. وقال أبو يوسف: هي بيع إلا إن تعذر بأن كانت قبل القبض ففسخ إلا إن تعذرا فتبطل بأن كانت قبل القبض في المنقول بأكثر من الثمن الأول أو بأقل منه أو بجنس آخر أو بعد هلاك المبيع. وقال محمد : هي فسخ إلا إن تعذر بأن تقايلا بأكثر من الثمن الأول أو بخلاف جنسه أو ولدت المبيعة بعد القبض فبيع إلا أن تعذرا بأن كانت قبل القبض بأكثر من الثمن الأول فتبطل. والخلاف المذكور إنما هو فيما إذا وقعت بلفظها، أما بلفظ الفسخ أو الرد أو الترك فإنها لا تكون بيعاً. وفي بعض نسخ الزيلعي فإنها لا تكون فسخاً وهو سبق قلم كما لا يخفى وفي السراج الوهاج : أما إذا كانت بلفظ البيع كانت بيعاً إجماعاً كما إذا قال البائع له بعني ما اشتريت فقال بعت كان بيعاً. وفائدة كونها فسخاً في حقهما عنده تظهر في خمس مسائل : الأولى وجوب رد الثمن الأول . قوله : (وإنما يضمن الوكيل بالبيع الخ) عبارة الظهيرية على ما رأيت فيها نصها: والوكيل بالبيع يملك الإقالة قبل قبض الثمن في قول محمد رحمه الله تعالى قوله : (لأن بإقالته يعود المبيع إلى ملك العاقد الخ) وجهه أن الإقالة بيع جديد في حق العاقدين فصارت البائعة وكيلة بالبيع بالإجازة لأن الإجازة اللاحقة كالوكالة السابقة، ثم لما أقالت البيع صارت مشترية لنفسها والشراء لا يتوقف متى وجد نفاذاً على العاقد فصار الشراء لها وإن أجاز الابن لعدم التوقف فإذا باعت ثانياً فقد باعت ملكها فلا يتوقف على إجازة الابن. قوله : أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان قبل القبض أو بعده) أي أطلق قوله هي وتسمية خلافه باطل الثانية أنها لا تبطل بالشروط المفسدة ولكن لا يصح تعليقها بالشرط كأن باع ثوراً من زيد فقال اشتريته رخيصاً فقال زيد إن وجدت مشترياً بالزيادة فبعه منه فوجد فباع بأزيد لا ينعقد البيع الثاني لأنه تعليق الإقالة لا الوكالة بالشرط؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج. الثالثة إذا تقايلا ولم يرد المبيع حتى باعه منه ثانياً جاز ولو كانت بيعاً لفسد، حجة على أبي يوسف لأن البيع جائز بلا خلاف بين أصحابنا إلا أن يثبت عنه الخلاف فيه؛ . وهذه كذا في البدائع ولو باع من غير المشتري لم يجز لكونه بيعاً جديداً في حق ثالث وإذا تبايعاه بعدها يحتاج المشتري إلى تجديد القبض لكونه بعدها في يده مضموناً بغيره وهو الثمن فلا ينوب عن قبض الشراء كقبض الرهن بخلاف قبض الغصب؛ كذا في الكافي هنا . وفيه من باب المتفرقات : تقايضا فتقايلا فاشترى أحدهما ما أقال صار قابضاً بنفس العقد لقيامهما فكان كل واحد مضموناً بقيمة نفسه كالمغصوب ولو هلك أحدهما فتقايلا ثم جدد العقد في القائم لا يصير قابضاً بنفس العقد لأنه يصير مضموناً بقيمة العرض الآخر فشابه المرهون اهـ . والرابعة إذا وهب المبيع من المشتري بعد الإقالة قبل القبض جازت الهبة، ولو كانت بيعاً لانفسخ لأن البيع ينفسخ بهبة المبيع للبائع قبل القبض. والخامسة لو كان المبيع مكيلاً أو موزوناً وقد باعه منه بالكيل أو الوزن ثم تقايلا واسترد المبيع من غير أن يعيد الكيل أو الوزن جاز قبضه وهذا لا يطرد على أصل أبي يوسف لكونها بيعاً عنده ولو كانت بيعاً لما صح قبضه بلا كبل ووزن ؛ كذا في البدائع. تظهر فائدة كونها بيعاً في حق غيرهما في خمس مسائل) قال في النهر : زاد في النهاية سادسة وهي ما مر من أن قبض بدلي الصرف شرط لصحة الإقالة فيجعل في حق الشرع كبيع جديد وسئلت عن الإقالة بعد الرهن فأجبت بأنها موقوفة كالبيع من قولهم إنها بيع جديد في حق ثالث وهو هنا المرتهن وهي سابعة وعلى هذا لو أجره ثم تقايلا فهي ثامنة اهـ. فالإقالة بعد الرهن موقوفة على إجازة المرتهن أو قضاء الراهن دينه وبعد الإجازة موقوفة على إجازة المستأجر إن أجاز نفذت وإلا بطلت ويزاد أيضاً ما نقله السيد الحموي عن ابن قرشته أخذاً لا وهو ما إذا اشترى جارية وقبضها ثم تقايلا البيع نزل هذا التقايل منزلة البيع في حق ثالث حتى يكون للبائع الأول وطؤها إلا بعد الاستبراء .اهـ. لأن وجوب الاستبراء حق الله تعالى فالله تعالى
His statement: (It is a rescission regarding the two contracting parties, a sale regarding a third party, and this is according to Abu Hanifa, unless making it a rescission is impossible, such as if the sold item gives birth after possession or is destroyed, then it becomes void and the sale remains as it was. He made it absolute, so it includes whether it is before or after possession, and it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that it is a rescission before that, in al-Badai‘. The apparent meaning is affirming the absolute. Abu Yusuf said: It is a sale unless it is impossible, such as if it is before possession, then it is a rescission unless both are impossible, then it becomes void, such as if it is before possession in movables for more than the first price, or less, or with a different type, or after the destruction of the sold item. Muhammad said: It is a rescission unless it is impossible, such as if they rescind for more than the first price, contrary to its type, or the sold item gives birth after possession, then it is a sale, unless both are impossible, such as if it is before possession for more than the first price, then it becomes void. The mentioned disagreement only applies when it occurs with its specific wording; but if it is with the wording of rescission, return, or abandonment, then it is not a sale. In some copies of al-Zayla‘i, it is not a rescission, but that is a slip of the pen, as is not hidden. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If it is with the wording of sale, it is a sale by consensus, as if the seller said to him: Sell me what I bought, and he said: I sold, it is a sale. The benefit of it being a rescission regarding the two parties according to him appears in five issues: the first is the obligation to return the first price. His statement: (And the agent for sale is only liable...) The wording of al-Zahiriya as I saw in it: The agent for sale has the right of rescission before receiving the price according to Muhammad. His statement: (Because by his rescission, the sold item returns to the ownership of the contracting party...) The explanation is that rescission is a new sale regarding the contracting parties, so the woman seller becomes an agent for sale by ratification because subsequent ratification is like prior agency. Then when she rescinded the sale, she became a buyer for herself, and purchase does not depend on the agent when found executed, so the purchase becomes for her even if the son ratified, due to lack of dependence. So when she sold a second time, she sold her own property, so it does not depend on the son's ratification. His statement: (He made it absolute so it includes whether it is before or after possession) meaning he made his statement absolute... and naming its disagreement is invalid. The second: that it is not invalidated by corrupting conditions, but its suspension upon a condition is not valid. The third: if they rescind and the sold item is not returned until he sells it to him a second time, it is valid; if it were a sale, it would be invalid, as a proof against Abu Yusuf. The fourth: if he gives the sold item as a gift to the buyer after rescission before possession, the gift is valid. The fifth: if the sold item is measured or weighed and he sold it by measure or weight, then they rescind and he takes back the sold item without repeating the measure or weight, his possession is valid. The benefit of it being a sale regarding others appears in five issues... Al-Nahr said: Al-Nihaya added a sixth... and he was asked about rescission after pledge, so he answered that it is suspended like a sale... and it is also added what al-Sayyid al-Hamawi quoted from Ibn Qarshata: if he bought a slave girl and possessed her, then they rescind the sale, this rescission takes the place of a sale regarding a third party so that the first seller has the right to have intercourse with her only after istibra‘.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the nature of iqalah (rescission of a sale). The author begins by explaining the statement in the matn: Iqalah is faskh (rescission) for the two contracting parties but a sale for a third party, according to Abu Hanifa. The exception is when making it faskh is impossible, e.g., if the sold animal gives birth or is destroyed after possession, then the iqalah becomes void and the original sale remains. This ruling applies whether iqalah is done before or after possession. Abu Yusuf holds that iqalah is a sale, unless it is before possession, in which case it is faskh; if both are impossible, it becomes void. Muhammad holds that iqalah is faskh, unless it is done for more than the original price or with a different genus, or if the item gives birth after possession, then it is a sale; if both are impossible, it becomes void. These differences only apply when iqalah is pronounced with its specific wording; if it uses words like faskh, radd, or tark, it is not a sale. The passage then lists five practical benefits of considering it faskh regarding the parties: (1) Only the first price must be returned, not a different one; (2) It is not invalidated by corrupt conditions but cannot be suspended on a condition; (3) If they rescind and the seller immediately resells to the same buyer without taking possession, it is valid; (4) If the seller gives the item as a gift to the buyer after iqalah but before possession, the gift is valid; (5) For measured/weighed items, once rescinded, the seller can take possession without re-measuring. The passage also lists benefits of considering it a sale regarding third parties, adding that iqalah after a pledge is suspended until the pledgee's consent, and iqalah of a slave girl gives the first seller the right to intercourse only after istibra‘. The author uses this analysis to prove that iqalah is not a simple rescission but has characteristics of a new sale in certain contexts.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqālah): Rescission of a sale by mutual agreement; it annuls the original sale but may be considered a new sale for third parties.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract ab initio as if it never existed.
بيع (bay‘): A contract of sale transferring ownership for a price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the nature of iqalah regarding the two contracting parties and a third party?
A) It is a sale for both parties and a third party.
B) It is a rescission for both parties and a sale for a third party.
C) It is a rescission for both parties and a third party.
D) It is a sale for the parties and a rescission for a third party.

Key Takeaway
Iqalah is a hybrid transaction: faskh for the parties but bay' for third parties (Abu Hanifa), with nuanced exceptions that affect practical rulings.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
8 min
The Dual Nature of Iqaalah: Faskh and Bai'
Explaining that iqaalah is rescission for the parties but a new sale for third parties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two natures of iqaalah and apply the dual nature to specific legal scenarios.

Core Idea
Iqaalah is rescission for the parties but a new sale for third parties — a dual nature with distinct consequences.

This lesson explores the Hanafi view on the legal nature of iqaalah (mutual rescission). Why does it matter? Because whether it is treated as a rescission or a new sale changes the rights and obligations of the parties and third parties. We will examine the evidence for the dual nature and its application in various case studies.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on iqaalah
Identify that iqaalah is faskh between the parties but bai' for third parties. This is the starting point.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Note Abu Yusuf's view that iqaalah is a sale in real estate. How does it differ from the majority?
Also note the narration from Abu Hanifa about qabd.
3
Examine the proofs from the text
The passage lists many examples (masail) that demonstrate the dual nature. Go through each one to see how the ruling changes depending on whether we apply faskh or bai'.
Focus on the gift, pre-emption, and possession examples.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
For each example, determine what the practical consequence is if we follow Abu Yusuf's view versus the majority.
This shows why the distinction matters.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position is the majority view: faskh between parties, bai' for third parties. Understand why this is preferred.
Al-Marghinani and others prefer this.

وتسمية خلافه باطل الثانية أنها لا تبطل بالشروط المفسدة ولكن لا يصح تعليقها بالشرط كأن باع ثوراً من زيد فقال اشتريته رخيصاً فقال زيد إن وجدت مشترياً بالزيادة فبعه منه فوجد فباع بأزيد لا ينعقد البيع الثاني لأنه تعليق الإقالة لا الوكالة بالشرط؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج. الثالثة إذا تقايلا ولم يرد المبيع حتى باعه منه ثانياً جاز ولو كانت بيعاً لفسد، حجة على أبي يوسف لأن البيع جائز بلا خلاف بين أصحابنا إلا أن يثبت عنه الخلاف فيه؛ كذا في البدائع ولو باع من غير المشتري لم يجز لكونه بيعاً جديداً في حق ثالث وإذا تبايعاه بعدها يحتاج المشتري إلى تجديد القبض لكونه بعدها في يده مضموناً بغيره وهو الثمن فلا ينوب عن قبض الشراء كقبض الرهن بخلاف قبض الغصب؛ كذا في الكافي هنا. وفيه من باب المتفرقات: تقايضا فتقايلا فاشترى أحدهما ما أقال صار قابضاً بنفس العقد لقيامهما فكان كل واحد مضموناً بقيمة نفسه كالمغصوب ولو هلك أحدهما فتقايلا ثم جدد العقد في القائم لا يصير قابضاً بنفس العقد لأنه يصير مضموناً بقيمة العرض الآخر فشابه المرهون اهـ. والرابعة إذا وهب المبيع من المشتري بعد الإقالة قبل القبض جازت الهبة، ولو كانت بيعاً لانفسخ لأن البيع ينفسخ بهبة المبيع للبائع قبل القبض. والخامسة لو كان المبيع مكيلاً أو موزوناً وقد باعه منه بالكيل أو الوزن ثم تقايلا واسترد المبيع من غير أن يعيد الكيل أو الوزن جاز قبضه وهذا لا يطرد على أصل أبي يوسف لكونها بيعاً عنده ولو كانت بيعاً لما صح قبضه بلا كيل ووزن؛ كذا في البدائع. تظهر فائدة كونها بيعاً في حق غيرهما في خمس مسائل
فسخ في حق المتعاقدين قال في المجتبى والإقالة قبل القبض في المنقول وغيره فسخ عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد، وكذا عند أبي يوسف في المنقول لتعذر البيع، وفي العقار تكون بيعاً عنده. ك: وعن الحسن عن أبي حنيفة بيع بعد القبض فسخ قبله إلا في العقار فإنه بيع فيهما قوله: (وظاهره) أي ظاهر التعبير بقوله «وروي». قوله: (وإذا تبايعاه (بعدها أي بعد الإقالة وهو بيان لقوله جاز أي جاز بيعه قبل رده ولكن يحتاج المشتري إلى قبض جديد وهذا فيما يتعين كونه مبيعاً كما يفيده ما سيذكره عن الكافي أيضاً. قوله: (تقايضا) من المقايضة فهو بالياء المثناة التحتية لا بالياء الموحدة وقوله لقيامهما أي قيام كل من عوضي المقايضة قوله: وتظهر فائدة كونها بيعاً في حق غيرهما في خمس مسائل) قال في النهر: زاد في النهاية سادسة وهي ما مر من أن قبض بدلي الصرف شرط لصحة الإقالة فيجعل في حق الشرع كبيع جديد وسئلت عن الإقالة بعد الرهن فأجبت بأنها موقوفة كالبيع من قولهم إنها بيع جديد في حق ثالث وهو هنا المرتهن وهي سابعة وعلى هذا لو أجره ثم تقايلا فهي ثامنة اهـ. فالإقالة بعد الرهن موقوفة على إجازة المرتهن أو قضاء الراهن دينه وبعد الإجازة موقوفة على إجازة المستأجر إن أجاز نفذت وإلا بطلت ويزاد أيضاً ما نقله السيد الحموي عن ابن قرشته
وهو ما إذا اشترى جارية وقبضها ثم تقايلا البيع نزل هذا التقايل منزلة البيع في حق ثالث حتى يكون للبائع الأول وطؤها إلا بعد الاستبراء. اهـ. لأن وجوب الاستبراء حق الله تعالى فالله تعالى

أيضاً: الأولى لو كان المبيع عقاراً فسلم الشفيع الشفعة ثم تقايلا يقضى له بالشفعة لكونه بيعاً جديداً في حقه كأنه اشتراه منه والثانية إذا باع المشتري المبيع من آخر ثم تقايلا ثم اطلع على كأن كان في يد بأقل من الثمن الأول جاز وكان في حتى البائع كالمملوك بشراء جديد من المشتري الثاني والرابعة إذا كان المبيع موهوباً فباعه الموهوب له ثم تقايلا ليس للواهب أن يرجع في هبته لأن الموهوب له في حق الواهب الدين حالاً كأنه باعه منه. وفي الصغرى ولو رده بعيب بقضاء كان فسخاً من كل وجه فيعود الأجل كما كان ولو كان بالدين كفيل لا تعود الكفالة في الوجهين اهـ. وكما إذا تقايلا ثم ادعى رجل أن المبيع ملكه وشهد المشتري بذلك لم تقبل شهادته لأنه هو الذي باعه ثم شهد أنه لغيره ولو كانت فسخاً لقبلت ألا ترى أن المشتري لو رد المبيع بعيب بقضاء وادعى المبيع رجل وشهد المشتري بذلك تقبل شهادته لأنه بالفسخ عاد ملكه القديم فلم يكن من جهة المشتري لكونه فسخاً من كل وجه. وكذا لو باع عبداً بطعام بغير عينه وقبض ثم تقايلا لا يتعين الطعام المقبوض للرد كأنه باعه من البائع بطعام غير معين، وكذا لو قبض الثمن الأول أو أجود منه يجب رد مثل المشروط في البيع الأول كأنه باعه من البائع متلقياً أردأ من ثالثهما؛ كذا في حاشية أبي السعود. قوله: (الأولى لو كان المبيع عقاراً فسلم الشفيع الشفعة الخ) قال الرملي: إنما قال «فسلم» لتظهر فائدة كونها بيعاً وإلا لو لم يسلم بأن أقال قبل أن يعلم الشفيع بالبيع فله الأخذ بالشفعة أيضاً إن شاء بالبيع الأول وإن شاء بالبيع الحاصل بالإقالة تأمل. قوله: (وفي الصغرى ولو رده بعيب الخ) قال الرملي صورة عبارة الصغرى ومن له دين مؤجل إذا اشترى بذلك الدين ممن عليه شيئاً وقبضه ثم تقايلا لا يعود الأجل ولو رده بعيب إلى آخر ما هنا وسيأتي في الكفالة عن التتارخانية ما يخالف ما هنا فراجعه وتأمل اهـ والذي الرملي هناك
Book of Sale, Chapter of Iqaalah. And naming its opposite is invalid. Second: it is not invalidated by corrupt conditions, but it is not valid to suspend it on a condition. Example: if someone sold an ox to Zayd and said 'I bought it cheaply,' and Zayd said 'If you find a buyer at a higher price, sell it to him,' and he found and sold at a higher price, the second sale is not concluded because it is a suspension of iqaalah, not agency by condition; as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. Third: if they mutually rescind and have not returned the sold item until he sells it to him again, it is permissible. If it were a sale, it would be invalid, as a proof against Abu Yusuf, because the sale is valid without disagreement among our companions unless the disagreement about it is established from him; as in al-Bada'i. And if he sells to someone other than the buyer, it is not permissible because it is a new sale in respect of a third party. And if they mutually sell after it, the buyer needs to renew the taking of possession because after it the item in his hand is guaranteed by something else, namely the price, so it does not substitute for taking possession of the purchase like taking possession of a pledge, unlike taking possession of a usurped item; as in al-Kafi here. And in it from the chapter of miscellaneous: they exchanged, then mutually rescinded, then one of them bought what he had rescinded; he becomes a possessor by the contract itself because both are standing, so each one is guaranteed by its own value like a usurped item. And if one of them perished and they mutually rescinded, then renewed the contract on the remaining, he does not become a possessor by the contract itself because it becomes guaranteed by the value of the other item, so it resembles the mortgaged item. End. Fourth: if after the iqaalah before taking possession the seller gives the sold item as a gift to the buyer, the gift is valid. If it were a sale, it would be annulled because a sale is annulled by a gift of the sold item to the seller before taking possession. Fifth: if the sold item is measured or weighed and he sold it to him by measure or weight, then they mutually rescinded and he took back the sold item without repeating the measure or weight, his taking possession is valid. And this does not follow according to Abu Yusuf's principle because it is a sale in his view, and if it were a sale, his taking possession would not be valid without measure or weight; as in al-Bada'i. The benefit of it being a sale in respect of others appears in five issues. It is rescission in respect of the two contracting parties. He said in al-Mujtaba: iqaalah before taking possession in movable and other is rescission according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and also according to Abu Yusuf in movable because a sale is impossible, but in real estate it is a sale according to him. K: From al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa: it is a sale after taking possession, rescission before it, except in real estate where it is a sale in both. His statement: (and its apparent) meaning the apparent expression of his saying 'and it is narrated'. His statement: (and if they mutually sell after it) i.e., after the iqaalah, and it is an explanation of his saying 'permissible', i.e., his sale before returning it is permissible, but the buyer needs a new taking of possession, and this is in what is determined to be the sold item, as will be mentioned from al-Kafi also. His statement: (they exchanged) from exchange, it is with ya mushaddad from below, not with ba mushaddad. His statement: (because both are standing) i.e., the standing of each of the two exchange items. His statement: (and the benefit of it being a sale in respect of others appears in five issues) He said in al-Nahr: al-Nihaya added a sixth, which is what has passed, that taking possession of the two exchange items in a currency exchange is a condition for the validity of iqaalah, so it is made in the view of the Law as a new sale. And I was asked about iqaalah after a pledge, and I answered that it is suspended like a sale, from their statement that it is a new sale in respect of a third party, and here it is the mortgagee, and it is a seventh. And on this, if he leased it then they mutually rescinded, it is an eighth. End. So iqaalah after a pledge is suspended on the permission of the mortgagee or the pledgor paying his debt, and after permission it is suspended on the permission of the lessee; if he permits it is executed, otherwise it is void. And there is added also what al-Sayyid al-Hamawi narrated from Ibn Qarashatah: if he bought a female slave and took possession of her, then they mutually rescinded the sale, this iqaalah is treated like a sale in respect of a third party, so that the first seller may have intercourse with her only after istibra'. End, because the obligation of istibra' is a right of Allah the Exalted, so Allah the Exalted... Also: First: if the sold item was real estate and the pre-emptor waived pre-emption, then they mutually rescinded, he is decreed pre-emption because it is a new sale in his right, as if he bought it from him. Second: if the buyer sold the sold item to another, then they mutually rescinded, then he discovered a defect while it was in the seller's hand, so he wanted to return it to the seller, he does not have that because it is in his right as if it is a sale he bought from the buyer. Third: if he bought something and took possession but did not pay the price until he sold it to another, then they mutually rescinded and it returned to the buyer, then he bought it before paying its price for less than the first price, it is permissible, and it is in relation to the seller like a property by a new purchase from the second buyer. Fourth: if the sold item was a gift and the donee sold it, then they mutually rescinded, the donor cannot revoke his gift because the donee in the donor's right is like a buyer from the buyer from him. Fifth: if he bought with trade goods a slave for service after a year had passed on them, then he found a defect in him and returned him without a court order and took back the trade goods, and they perished in his hand, then his zakat is not waived because it is a new sale in respect of a third party, namely the poor person, because return by defect without a court order is iqaalah. And his statement 'new sale in respect of a third party' is applied absolutely, and his statement 'rescission in respect of the two contracting parties' is not applied absolutely, because it is only rescission in what is from the implications of the contract, i.e., what is established by the contract itself without a condition. But if it is not from the implications of the contract and is obligatory due to an additional condition, then by iqaalah it is considered a new sale in respect of the two contracting parties as well, as when he bought an item with deferred debt before the term, then they mutually rescinded, the debt becomes due immediately, as if he sold it to him. And in al-Sughra: and if he returned it by defect with a court order, it is rescission from every aspect, so the term returns as it was, and if there was a guarantor for the debt, the guarantee does not return in both cases. End. And as when they mutually rescinded, then a man claimed that the sold item belonged to him and the buyer testified to that, his testimony is not accepted because he is the one who sold it, then testified that it is for another. If it were a rescission, it would be accepted. Do you not see that if the buyer returned the sold item by defect with a court order, and a man claimed the sold item and the buyer testified to that, his testimony is accepted because by the rescission his old ownership returned, so it was not from the side of the buyer because it is a rescission from every aspect. And likewise if he sold a slave by unspecified food and took possession, then they mutually rescinded, the taken food is not specified for return, as if he sold it to the seller by unspecified food. And likewise if he took back the first price or a better one, he must return the like of what was stipulated in the first sale, as if he sold it to the seller receiving inferior from the third of them; as in Hashiyat Abi al-Su'ud. His statement: (First: if the sold item was real estate and the pre-emptor waived pre-emption, etc.) Al-Ramli said: He only said 'waived' to show the benefit of it being a sale; otherwise if he did not waive, i.e., he made iqaalah before the pre-emptor knew of the sale, then he may also take by pre-emption if he wishes, either by the first sale or by the sale resulting from the iqaalah. Consider. His statement: (And in al-Sughra: and if he returned it by defect, etc.) Al-Ramli said: The wording of al-Sughra is: Someone who has a deferred debt, if he buys with that debt from the one who owes it something and takes possession, then they mutually rescind, the term does not return. And if he returns it by defect up to what is here, and in al-Kafalah from al-Tatarakhaniyya there will be something that contradicts what is here, so refer to it and consider. End. And what will come in al-Kafalah is his statement: if the principal seller sold the claimant for his debt, it drops. So if he returned it by a new ownership, the debt returns on the principal seller but does not return on the guarantor, and by rescission from every aspect it returns on the guarantor. End. So this contradicts his statement 'the guarantee does not return'. And al-Ramli mentioned there.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a comprehensive discussion of the legal nature of iqaalah (mutual rescission of sale) in the Hanafi school. The core issue is whether iqaalah is a rescission (faskh) or a new sale (bai'). The passage shows that it is faskh between the two contracting parties but treated as a new sale in the rights of third parties. This distinction has numerous practical consequences. The passage lists many examples (masail) to illustrate these consequences. It also records an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider iqaalah faskh in all cases, while Abu Yusuf holds that it is a sale in real estate (and in some other contexts). The relied-upon position is that iqaalah is faskh between the parties but bai' for others. The commentary explains each example, showing how the ruling differs depending on whether iqaalah is considered faskh or bai'. For instance, if the sold item is returned by iqaalah before taking possession, the buyer can give it as a gift (valid because it is faskh), whereas if it were a sale the gift would annul the sale. Similarly, the pre-emptor's rights, the return of the debt's term, and the effect on guarantees are all affected. The passage is from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq and incorporates glosses from various authorities, presenting a thorough analysis of the topic.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-Iqaalah): Mutual rescission of a sale contract by agreement of both parties.
فسخ (Faskh): Annulment of a contract, returning the parties to their pre-contract state.
بيع (Bai'): Sale; a contract transferring ownership for a price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the nature of iqaalah (mutual rescission) between the contracting parties?
A) It is always a new sale (bai').
B) It is always a rescission (faskh) in all respects.
C) It is a rescission (faskh) between the parties but a new sale (bai') in respect of third parties.
D) It is invalid unless done before taking possession.

Key Takeaway
Iqaalah is faskh between the parties but bai' for third parties; this dual nature governs all its consequences in Hanafi fiqh.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

The Dual Nature of Iqalah: New Sale for Third Parties vs. Rescission Between Parties
Clarifying the dual nature of iqalah with illustrative cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that iqalah is a new sale for third parties and a rescission only for inherent contract terms between parties, and apply this rule to cases involving pre-emption, resale, gift, zakat, and testimony.

Core Idea
Iqalah is a new sale for third parties and a rescission only for inherent contract terms between the parties.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqā’iq, clarifies a fundamental Hanafi principle: iqalah (mutual rescission of a sale) is not merely a rescission ab initio. Instead, it wields a dual nature—acting as a new sale regarding third parties (e.g., pre-emptors, witnesses) and as a rescission between the original parties only for obligations inherent to the contract itself. The author presents five concrete cases and several refinements to demonstrate how this distinction operates in practice.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the General Rule
Iqalah is a new sale (bay‘ jadīd) in the right of third parties and a rescission (faskh) between the contracting parties only for matters that are inherent requirements of the contract (muqtaḍā al-‘aqd).
This dual nature is the core principle to apply in all cases.
2
Work Through the Five Illustrative Cases
The text gives five scenarios (pre-emption, buyer reselling, deferred payment, gift, zakat) that each test the rule. For each, determine: who is the third party? How does classifying iqalah as a new sale affect their rights?
Case 1: Pre-emptor waived, then iqalah → pre-emption revives. Case 2: Buyer resold, then iqalah → original seller is a third party, so return for defect is disallowed. Case 3: Buyer resold without paying first price, then iqalah and repurchase at lower price → valid as new purchase from second buyer. Case 4: Donee sold gift, then iqalah → donor cannot revoke gift. Case 5: Return of defective slave without court order (iqalah) → zakat not dropped.
3
Identify the Exception for Non-Inherent Terms
When iqalah affects matters that require an additional condition (e.g., acceleration of a deferred debt), it is treated as a new sale even between the parties. The example: buying with deferred debt, then iqalah → debt becomes due immediately.
This shows the general statement 'rescission between parties' is not absolute.
4
Analyze the Testimony Case
After iqalah, if the buyer testifies that the sold item belongs to a third party, his testimony is rejected—because iqalah is a new sale, he is a seller, and a seller cannot be a witness for the buyer. In contrast, if the return was by court order (true rescission), his testimony would be accepted.
This starkly illustrates the difference between iqalah (new sale) and judicial rescission.
5
Apply to Unspecified Food and Price Equivalence
If a slave was sold for unspecified food and then iqalah occurs, the food does not become specified for return; it remains as if a new sale for unspecified food. Similarly, if the seller received the first price or a better one, he must return the equivalent, not the exact item.
These cases reinforce that iqalah is a new sale even between parties for non-inherent terms.

Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq explaining the dual nature of iqalah (rescission of sale). The author establishes that iqalah is considered a new sale (بَيْعٌ جَدِيدٌ) in relation to third parties, but a rescission (فَسْخٌ) between the two original contracting parties. He provides five illustrative cases. First, in the case of pre-emption (shuf'ah): if the pre-emptor waived his right and then the parties rescind, the pre-emptor regains the right because iqalah is a new sale from the seller to the buyer. Second, if the buyer resold the item and then rescinded, he cannot return it to the original seller for a defect because in the seller's regard it is as if he bought it from the buyer (a new sale). Third, if the buyer resold without paying the first price and then rescinded and repurchased for less, it is valid as a new purchase from the second buyer. Fourth, in a gift: if the donee sold the gift and then rescinded, the donor cannot revoke the gift because the donee is like a buyer from the buyer (i.e., a new sale). Fifth, regarding zakat: if one bought a slave for service using trade goods on which zakat was due, and then returned the slave for a defect without court order (which is considered iqalah), the zakat is not dropped because the return is a new sale in the sight of the third party (the poor). The author then refines the general statement: iqalah is rescission only in matters that are inherent requirements of the contract (موجبات العقد), but if a matter requires an additional condition (like the acceleration of a debt), iqalah is treated as a new sale even between the parties. He proves this by analyzing testimonies: if the buyer testifies after iqalah that the item belongs to another, his testimony is rejected because he sold it (evidence of a new sale). But if the return was by court order (a true rescission), his testimony would be accepted. The cases of food for slave and price also illustrate the same principle.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqālah): Rescission or cancellation of a sale contract by mutual agreement of the parties.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission; annulment of a contract ab initio as if it never existed.
بيع جديد (bay' jadīd): A new sale; the re-transfer of ownership considered as a separate contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, after iqalah (mutual rescission), if a third party claims the sold item and the buyer testifies that it belongs to that third party, what is the ruling on the buyer’s testimony?
A) The testimony is accepted because iqalah rescinds ownership fully.
B) The testimony is not accepted because iqalah is a new sale and the buyer is considered a seller.
C) The testimony is accepted only if the buyer returns the item with a court order.
D) The testimony is not accepted because the buyer’s ownership never ceased.

Key Takeaway
Iqalah is a new sale for third parties and a rescission only for inherent contract terms between the parties; this distinction governs pre-emption, defect returns, gift revocation, zakat, and testimony.

Iqalah with Changed Price: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on price conditions in iqalah and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iqalah with a changed price and differentiate the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Iqalah is a rescission, so it must return the same price; any condition of increase or decrease (without defect) is void.

This lesson delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding iqalah (mutual rescission of a sale). The core ruling from the matn is clear: iqalah is valid only with the same price as the original contract. But what happens if the parties stipulate a different price? Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani offer nuanced answers that affect whether the transaction remains a rescission or becomes a new sale. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to classify contracts and apply the mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
Identify the ruling from the matn: iqalah is valid only with the like of the first price; a condition of more or less (without a defect) is void, and the original price must be paid.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the variant views
Abu Yusuf holds that conditioning any difference (more or less) makes the iqalah a new sale. Muhammad distinguishes: conditioning more makes it a sale (impossible to rescind with more), conditioning less keeps it as a rescission with the first price.
Both Sahibayn differ from Abu Hanifa.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Abu Hanifa's dalil: rescission (faskh) returns to the same basis as the original contract – stipulating otherwise is invalid. Abu Yusuf's view: iqalah is fundamentally a new sale, so price changes are allowed. Muhammad's view: rescission is not possible with an increase, so it becomes a sale; a decrease is possible and remains rescission.
Each imam's principle leads to a different classification.
4
Apply the mufta bihi
In practice, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's: if parties stipulate a different price, the condition is void, and the original price is due. The iqalah remains valid as a rescission.
This is the fatwa for Hanafis today.
5
Note exceptions and branches
If the item becomes defective after sale, a condition of lesser price is allowed, and the reduction must equal the share of the defect. Also, if the price genus changes, the condition is void, and the original genus must be returned.
These details are derived from Fath al-Qadir and al-Binaya.

Source Text

And in al-Binaya attributed to Taj al-Shari'ah: This is when the share of the defect is equal to the reduced amount, or more, or less by an amount that people tolerate in bargaining. He restricted it by his saying 'another genus' because iqalah on a genus other than the first price is valid, the specified [price] is void, and he is obliged to return the first. So his saying 'and genus' is grammatically in the genitive case, coordinated with 'the more' meaning 'and the condition of genus' not 'on defect'. And according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, if he conditions more, it is a sale because that is the principle in it according to Abu Yusuf, and due to the impossibility of rescission according to Muhammad. Similarly, in conditioning the less, according to Abu Yusuf it is valid as a sale, and according to Muhammad it is rescission with the first price. And if the author had said 'and it is valid when silent about the first price' it would be better, so that the ruling when explicitly stated is known by priority. And when silent, there is no disagreement on the obligation of the first price, as in al-Bada'i'. And he indicated by his saying 'he is obliged to pay the first price' that the consideration is based on what the contract was made, because of what preceded. So in Fath al-Qadir: If the price was ten dinars and he gave him dirhams instead of dinars, then they rescinded while the dirhams had fallen in value, he returns the dinars on which the contract was made, not what he paid. Similarly, if returned by defect. Similarly, in rental if rescinded. And among the branches of rescission like iqalah: if they contracted with dirhams then they became demonetized, then they rescind, he returns those demonetized dirhams. And if they contracted with dirhams then renewed with dinars and vice versa, the first is rescinded. Similarly, if they contracted with a deferred price then renewed with a spot price or vice versa. As for if they renewed with dirhams more or less, there is no reduction [of the first price] and it is [considered] either from the price or an addition to it. And they said: If one sold for twelve and deducted two dirhams, then they made a contract for ten, the first is not rescinded because it is like it, since the deduction attaches to the original contract, except in oaths: he would be in violation if he had sworn not to buy it for twelve. And if the buyer after the contract before taking possession said to the seller 'Sell it to me', it will come in al-Khaniyyah at the beginning of the Chapter on Disposal of the Sold Item, restricted by when he did not...
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of iqalah (rescission) with a price different from the original. The matn states that iqalah is valid only with the same price (مِثْل الثَّمَنِ الأَوَّل); any condition of more or less (without a defect in the item) is void, and the original price remains due. The same applies to a different genus of price. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that rescission (faskh) reverts to the same terms as the original contract; therefore, stipulating a different price is invalid, but the iqalah itself remains valid. The exception is when the item has become defective after the sale: then a condition of a lesser price is permissible, and the reduction should correspond to the loss in value. The restriction 'without defect' ensures that a post-sale defect justifies a price reduction. The commentator cites Fath al-Qadir and al-Binaya for details on the extent of reduction. The intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold different views. According to Abu Yusuf, conditioning a higher price makes the iqalah a new sale (since that is his principle for iqalah), and conditioning a lower price also makes it a sale. According to Muhammad, if the condition is for more, it is impossible to rescind with more, so it becomes a sale; if the condition is for less, it is still a rescission with the first price. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view, which is the relied-upon position in the school. The passage also discusses related issues: when the price is not explicitly mentioned (silence), the first price is due by consensus; examples of price changes (currency devaluation, renewal of contract), and the effect of deducing a discount in the original contract. The last part mentions a case from al-Khaniyyah about the buyer asking the seller to resell after contract.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqālah): Rescission of a sale contract by mutual consent; in this context, a voluntary cancellation.
ثمن (thaman): Price; the consideration paid for the sold item.
عيب ('ayb): Defect in the sold item that gives the buyer the right to return it.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission; annulment of a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), if parties stipulate a lower price in iqalah without any defect, what is the ruling?
A) The iqalah is invalid.
B) The condition is void, and the seller must return the original price.
C) The condition is valid, and the lower price applies.
D) The iqalah becomes a new sale at the lower price.

Key Takeaway
Iqalah is a rescission, so it must revert to the original price; a change in price without a defect is void, and the original price is due.

Iqalah with Altered Price: Intra-Hanafi Dispute Resolved
Commentary on iqalah with intra-Hanafi dispute on price alteration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's ruling on iqalah when a different price is stipulated, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Iqalah is rescission, not a new sale — a condition changing the price is void, and the original price is binding.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a key issue in iqalah: can the parties agree to rescind at a price different from the original? The text presents the position of Imam Abu Hanifa, who insists that iqalah is a rescission that must mirror the original contract, and then introduces the dissenting views of his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to identify which opinion is operative for fatwa and why rescission cannot be used to alter contractual terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is the act being discussed? (Iqalah with a condition of different price). According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling? (Iqalah valid, condition void, original price due).
2
Locate the dalil
What is Abu Hanifa's proof? (Rescission returns upon the defect of what the contract returned upon; i.e., it must mirror the original terms).
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
How do the Sahibayn differ? (Abu Yusuf: iqalah becomes a new sale; Muhammad: if more, new sale; if less, rescission with original price).
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence? (Under Abu Hanifa, original price due; under Sahibayn, the stipulated price may apply in some cases).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Which position is relied upon? (Abu Hanifa's view; the commentary structures the ruling around it, and it is the zahir al-riwayah position).

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on iqalah (mutual rescission of a sale) in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It begins with the matn (core text) stating that stipulating a lower price without a defect or a different currency is void, and the original price is binding. The commentary then expands on this. The author first discusses the scenario where the parties agree to rescind at a price different from the original (more, less, or different currency). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, iqalah is a rescission (faskh) that mirrors the original contract. Therefore, any condition altering the price is void, and the original price remains due. The iqalah itself is valid with the original price. The commentator then presents the views of the two students (Sahibayn). Abu Yusuf holds that if a different price is stipulated, the transaction becomes a new sale (bay'), because for him iqalah is fundamentally a sale. Muhammad al-Shaybani distinguishes: if the stipulated price is more, it is a sale because rescission is impossible; if less, it remains a rescission with the first price. The commentary also touches on the exception for when the commodity has developed a defect after the contract, allowing a reduction to compensate for the defect, provided the reduction does not exceed the value lost. Later, the author discusses practical applications, such as when the currency changes value or when the price was deferred, emphasizing that the original price is always the reference. The passage also includes a brief discussion on the guarantor's liability in case of return due to defect, indicating a difference of opinion among later scholars.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqalah): Mutual rescission of a sale contract with the consent of both parties.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract reverting to the original state.
التعيب (al-ta'yib): The occurrence of a defect in the sold item after the contract but before iqalah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The iqalah is valid but the original price remains due, the condition is void.
D) The iqalah is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Iqalah is a rescission that mirrors the original contract; any condition changing the price is void, and the original price must be returned.

Destruction of the Sold Item Prevents Iqalah
Commentary on ruling with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when the sold item is destroyed before or after iqalah but before delivery, and apply the principle that existence of the item is a condition for iqalah.

Core Idea
If the sold item is destroyed before delivery, iqalah is invalid — existence is a condition for rescission.

When parties agree to rescind a sale (iqalah), the sold item must still exist and be capable of return. This lesson examines what happens if the item is destroyed — whether before or after the agreement but before delivery. The Hanafi position is clear: destruction invalidates the iqalah, and the original sale remains in effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is iqalah (mutual rescission of sale). The ruling is that it is invalid (batil) if the sold item is destroyed before delivery.
2
Understand the condition
A key condition for iqalah is that the sold item exists and can be returned. Destruction removes this possibility.
Existence is a condition (shart) for validity.
3
Trace the proof
The proof is derived from the principle that iqalah is not complete without possession (qabd). The text states: 'It is not completed except with possession.'
Wajh al-dalalah: qabd requires the item to exist.
4
Note the unanimous ruling
Multiple sources (al-Bazzaziyyah, Majma' al-Fatawa, Sharh al-Tahawi) confirm that destruction after iqalah but before delivery invalidates it. There is no intra-Hanafi dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وهلاك المبيع يمنع أي صحتها لما قدمنا أن من شرطها بقاء المبيع لأنها رفع . يقل له نعم فراجعه قول المصنف : (وهلاك المبيع يمنع قال الرملي :أقول : وكذا هلاكه بعد الإقالة وقبل التسليم يبطلها قال في البزازية: هلاك المبيع بعد الإقالة قبل التسليم مبطل وفي مجمع الفتاوى، ولو تقايلا ثم هلك المبيع قبل التسليم بطلت الإقالة وفي مجمع الرواية شرح القدوري قال في شرح الطحاوي: أو هلك المبيع بعد الإقالة قبل التسليم إلى البائع بطلت الإقالة، ومثله في كثير الكتب، ووجهه مأخوذ من قولهم لا تتم إلا بالقبض . قال في الخلاصة: ولو جاء المشتري إلى البائع وقال إنه قام علي بثمن غال فرد عليه البائع ما قبض من الثمن ولكن لم يقبض ما باع لا تتم الإقالة والشرط الإعطاء من الجانبين .اهـ. ولتمامها حكم إنشائها فكما لا يجوز إنشاؤها بعد هلاك المبيع فكذا هلاكه يبطلها. وقدم هذا الشارح في قوله هي فسخ أنه إذا تعذر جعلها فسخاً بأن ولدت المبيعة بعد القبض أو هلك المبيع فإنها تبطل ويبقى البيع على حاله والله تعالى أعلم اهـ. قلت : وما من
He said: (And destruction of the sold item prevents) i.e., its validity, because we have established that among its conditions is the existence of the sold item, as it is a lifting (of the contract). One may say to him yes; refer to it. The author said: (And destruction of the sold item prevents). Al-Ramli said: I say: Similarly, its destruction after iqalah and before delivery invalidates it. He said in al-Bazzaziyyah: Destruction of the sold item after iqalah before delivery is invalidating. In Majma' al-Fatawa, if they call off and then the sold item is destroyed before delivery, the iqalah is invalidated. In Majma' al-Riwayah, Sharh al-Quduri: He said in Sharh al-Tahawi: If the sold item is destroyed after iqalah before delivery to the seller, the iqalah is invalidated. Similar is in many books, and its reasoning is taken from their saying: It is not completed except with possession. He said in al-Khulasah: If the buyer comes to the seller and says that he has been charged a high price, and the seller returns what he took of the price, but does not take possession of what he sold, the iqalah is not completed, and the condition is giving from both sides. And for its completion, it is like its initiation: just as it is not permissible to initiate it after the destruction of the sold item, similarly its destruction invalidates it. And the commentator presented earlier in his saying that it is rescission that if it is impossible to make it rescission, such as if the sold female gives birth after possession or the sold item is destroyed, then it is invalidated and the sale remains as it was. And Allah knows best. He said: And what from...
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage addresses the condition that the sold item must exist for iqalah to be valid. The commentator begins by quoting the matn: 'Destruction of the sold item prevents' iqalah, meaning it prevents its validity, because a condition of iqalah is that the item is still in existence so it can be returned. The commentary then expands to a discussion that if the item is destroyed after iqalah but before delivery to the seller, the iqalah is also invalidated. This is based on the principle that iqalah is not complete until both parties have given and taken (i.e., the buyer returns the item and the seller returns the price). The commentator cites multiple authorities: al-Bazzaziyyah, Majma' al-Fatawa, Sharh al-Tahawi, and al-Khulasah, all confirming that destruction after iqalah but before delivery voids the iqalah. The reasoning is derived from the rule that iqalah requires possession (qabd). The passage also mentions that if the buyer comes to the seller complaining of a high price and the seller returns the price but does not take back the item, the iqalah is not complete. This emphasizes that iqalah requires mutual surrender of both the price and the item. Finally, the commentator connects this to the earlier statement that iqalah is a rescission (faskh): if rescission becomes impossible due to destruction or a birth, the iqalah is invalid and the original sale remains in effect.

Key Terms
الهلاك (al-halak): Complete destruction of the sold item, rendering it impossible to return.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking physical possession; a condition for the completion of iqalah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the parties agree to an iqalah (mutual rescission) but before the buyer returns the sold item to the seller, the item is accidentally destroyed. What is the ruling?
A) The iqalah is still valid; the buyer must pay the price.
B) The iqalah is invalidated, and the original sale remains in effect.
C) The iqalah is valid only if the buyer had already received the price.
D) The iqalah becomes a sale of the destroyed item.

Key Takeaway
Existence of the sold item and its delivery to the seller are essential conditions for a valid iqalah; destruction before delivery voids the iqalah and preserves the original sale.

Core Rulings on Iqaalah (Rescission of Sale)
Compilation of fatwa-style rulings on iqaalah and related contract issues

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on iqaalah, including when it rescinds a sale, the effect of denial, and the procedure for perishable goods.

Core Idea
Iqaalah rescinds a sale like a new contract; denial of sale acts as rescission except in marriage.

Iqaalah is the mutual agreement to rescind a sale. The Hanafi school treats it as a rescission (فسخ), not a new sale, with specific rules. This lesson draws from the Bahr al-Raiq compilation to clarify when iqaalah is valid, how denial of a sale affects the contract, and special provisions for perishable goods and disputed claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of iqaalah
Understand that iqaalah is a rescission, not a new sale, and that it is valid as long as the sold item exists. Note the general principle: if the buyer sells the item to a third party, the first sale is rescinded.
The text begins with 'If he sold it, it is valid and the first (sale) is rescinded.'
2
Analyze the effect of denial (juhood)
Denial of a contract other than marriage is considered a rescission. This affects the permissibility of intercourse with a slave-girl: if either party denies the sale, the seller may not have intercourse unless litigation is abandoned.
The text cites al-Fatawa al-Sughra: 'Denial of anything other than marriage is a rescission.'
3
Apply the rule for perishable goods
When the buyer is absent and the seller fears spoilage, the seller may sell the goods to another by istihsan. The seller gives in charity any excess over the original price, and bears any loss.
This is an exception by istihsan to the general prohibition of selling before delivery.
4
Determine the consequences of disputes
If the parties differ over whether iqaalah occurred, the default rule is that the word of the buyer is taken if he denies iqaalah; if the roles are reversed, both take oaths. Also, any deferment of the price after iqaalah is void per Abu Hanifa.
The text references Fath and Qunyah for these rulings.
5
Identify liability for expenses
If the buyer incurs expenses (carriage, maintenance) after purchase and then iqaalah occurs, the seller must bear the cost of returning the item.
From al-Qunyah: 'expenses of return are upon the seller.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن باعه جاز وانفسخ الأول، ولو قال بعه لي أو لم يزد على قوله بعه لي أو زاد قوله ممن شئت يصح في الوجوه لأنه توكيل ولو باع المبيع من البائع قبل القبض لا ينفسخ البيع ولو وهبه قبل القبض انفسخ إذا قبل ولو قال البائع قبل القبض أعتقه فأعتقه جاز العتق عن البائع وانفسخ البيع عند أبي حنيفة، وعند أبي يوسف العتق باطل. وفي الفتاوى الصغرى : جحود ما عدا النكاح فسخ وعليه ما فرع في الخانية وغيرها : باع أمة فأنكر المشتري الشراء لا يحل للبائع وطؤها إلا إن عزم على ترك الخصومة فيحل حينئذ له وطؤها، وكذا لو أنكر البائع البيع والمشتري يدعي لا يحل للبائع وطؤها، فإن ترك المشتري الخصومة وسمع البائع بعد بذلك حل له وطؤها. ومثله لو اشترى جارية بشرط الخيار ثلاثة أيام وقبضها ثم رد على البائع جارية أخرى في أيام الخيار وقال هي التي اشتريتها وقبضتها كان القول له، فإن رضي البائع بها حل وطؤها. وكذا القصار والإسكاف، وكذا لو اشترى ما يتسارع إليه الفساد كاللحم والسمك والفاكهة وغاب والمشتري وخاف البائع فساده فله بيعه من غيره استحساناً، وللمشتري منه الانتفاع به وإن علم لرضا العاقدين بالفسخ ظاهراً ويتصدق البائع بما زاد على الثمن، وإن نقص فعلى البائع ولو اختلفا فادعى البائع الإقالة والمشتري أنه باعه منه قبل النقد فالقول للمشتري في إنكارها ولو كان على العكس تحالفا؛ كذا في فتح وأشار أيضاً بقوله «الزمه الثمن الأول» إلى أنه لو كان الثمن الأول حالاً فأجله المشتري عند الإقالة فإن التأجيل يبطل وتصح الإقالة وإن تقايلا ثم أجله فينبغي أن لا يصح الأجل عند أبي حنيفة فإن الشرط اللاحق بعد العقد يلتحق بأصل العقد عنده ؛ كذا في القنية. وإلى أنه لو أبرأ المشتري عن الثمن بعد قبض المبيع ثم تقايلا لم تصح منها أيضاً، وإلى أنه يلزم المشتري رد المبيع. وفي القنية : اشترى ماله حمل ومؤنة ونقله إلى موضع آخر ثم تقايلا فمؤنة الرد على البائع اهـ .
If he sold it, it is valid and the first (sale) is rescinded. And if he said 'Sell it for me' or did not exceed saying 'Sell it for me' or added 'from whomever you wish', it is valid in all aspects because it is an agency. And if he sold the sold item to the seller before delivery, the sale is not rescinded. And if he gifted it before delivery, it is rescinded if accepted. And if the seller said before delivery 'I free him' and he freed him, the manumission is valid from the seller and the sale is rescinded according to Abu Hanifa; according to Abu Yusuf, the manumission is void. And in al-Fatawa al-Sughra: Denial of anything other than marriage is a rescission. And upon it, what is derived in al-Khaniyya and others: If he sold a slave-girl, and the buyer denied the purchase, it is not permissible for the seller to have intercourse with her unless he resolves to abandon litigation, then it becomes permissible for him to have intercourse with her. Similarly, if the seller denied the sale and the buyer claims (it), it is not permissible for the seller to have intercourse with her. If the buyer abandons litigation and the seller hears of that afterwards, intercourse with her becomes permissible for him. And similarly if he bought a slave-girl with a condition of option for three days and took possession, then returned another slave-girl to the seller during the option days and said 'This is the one I bought and took possession', his statement is accepted; if the seller is pleased with her, intercourse with her is permissible. And similarly the fuller and the shoemaker. And similarly if he bought something that quickly perishes like meat, fish, and fruit, and the buyer is absent, and the seller fears its perishing, he has the right to sell it to another by istihsan, and the buyer may benefit from it even if he knows, due to the apparent consent of the parties to rescission, and the seller gives in charity what exceeds the price, and if there is a loss, it is on the seller. And if they differ, and the seller claims iqaalah and the buyer claims that he sold it to him before payment, the statement is of the buyer in denial of it. If it were the opposite, they take an oath (each). As in Fath. And he also indicated by his saying 'The first price is binding' that if the first price was payable immediately and the buyer deferred it at the time of iqaalah, then the deferment is void and the iqaalah is valid. And if they did iqaalah and then he deferred it, it is appropriate that the deferment is not valid according to Abu Hanifa, because a condition that follows the contract is attached to the original contract according to him; as in al-Qunyah. And it indicates that if the buyer was discharged from the price after taking possession of the sold item and then they did iqaalah, it is not valid from her either, and it indicates that the buyer is required to return the sold item. And in al-Qunyah: If he bought something that has carrying expenses and maintenance and transported it to another place and then they did iqaalah, the expenses of return are upon the seller.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq compiles several rulings related to iqaalah (rescission of sale). It begins with a scenario: if a buyer sells the same item to a third party, the first sale is rescinded. Then it discusses agency in sale. The main part covers the manumission of a sold slave before delivery: Abu Hanifa rules that the manumission is valid and rescinds the sale, while Abu Yusuf says the manumission is void. The text then addresses the effect of denying a sale on the permissibility of intercourse (with a slave-girl). It provides specific rules: if either party denies the sale, the seller may not have intercourse unless litigation is abandoned. Similar rules apply for sale with option. The text also gives the seller the right to sell perishable goods to another by istihsan, with the excess given in charity. Disputes about iqaalah are resolved by taking oaths. Additionally, if the price is deferred after iqaalah, the deferment is invalid per Abu Hanifa. Finally, expenses of return are on the seller.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqaalah): Rescission of a sale contract by mutual agreement
جحود (juhood): Denial of a contract or claim
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference, a method of reasoning that overrides qiyas for a more equitable result

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the sale of perishable goods by the seller when the buyer is absent and spoilage is feared?
A) The seller must wait for the buyer's return.
B) The seller may sell the goods to another by istihsan and must give any profit in charity.
C) The seller may sell but keep the profit.
D) The buyer is liable for any loss.

Key Takeaway
Iqaalah rescinds a sale by mutual consent; denial of sale acts as rescission (except marriage), and special rules apply to perishable goods and disputed claims.

Manumission and Denial in Iqaalah: Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Compiles multiple fatwa-style rulings on iqaalah and related contract issues (focus on manumission and denial)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on manumission of a sold slave before delivery, and state the ruling on denial of sale affecting intercourse.

Core Idea
For Abu Hanifa, manumission before delivery rescinds the sale; for Abu Yusuf, it does not.

This lesson focuses on two specific issues within the broader topic of iqaalah: the manumission of a sold slave before delivery and the effect of denial of sale on the permissibility of intercourse. These rulings highlight an intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf and the application of the principle that denial acts as rescission.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the dispute
The scenario: a seller says 'I free him' regarding a sold slave before delivery. Abu Hanifa holds that the manumission is valid and the sale is rescinded. Abu Yusuf says the manumission is void and the sale remains.
The text explicitly presents both views.
2
Understand the underlying principle
Abu Hanifa's view aligns with treating iqaalah as rescission: manumission by the seller before delivery is an act that contradicts the continuation of the sale. Abu Yusuf might argue that the seller no longer owns the slave (ownership transferred at contract) so manumission is ineffective.
The passage does not explain the reasoning, but we can infer.
3
Apply the rule of denial (juhood)
Denial of sale (other than marriage) is a rescission. This has a direct effect on intercourse with a slave-girl: if either party denies the sale, the seller may not have intercourse unless litigation is abandoned.
The text provides detailed cases.
4
Analyze the case of option (khiyar)
When a slave-girl is bought with a condition of option for three days, and the buyer returns a different slave-girl claiming it is the same, his statement is accepted; if the seller accepts, intercourse becomes permissible.
This illustrates the principle that denial or return of a substitute does not automatically rescind; acceptance is needed.
5
Note the thamarah (practical consequence)
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, manumission frees the slave and the seller must refund the price; under Abu Yusuf, the manumission is void, so the slave remains enslaved and the sale continues.
This impacts the rights of the slave and the parties.
