Condition of Existence for Iqaalah: The Sold Item Must Be Present
Commentary on the condition of existence for iqaalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition that the sold item must exist for a valid iqaalah and apply this ruling to cases of total and partial destruction.

Core Idea
Iqaalah requires the sold item to exist; if it perishes, the sale continues.

Iqaalah (mutual rescission) is a way to undo a sale by consent. But it is not always possible—the sold item must still be in existence. This lesson explains why that condition is essential, what happens if the item is destroyed before or after the iqaalah agreement, and how partial destruction or barter sales affect the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its condition
Recognize that iqaalah is a rescission of a sale, and its validity depends on the existence of the sold item (mabee') at the time of the agreement.
This condition distinguishes iqaalah from a new sale, which can be made even if the item is destroyed (by agreeing on damages).
2
Understand the rationale
Iqaalah is a removal (رفع) of the contract. If the subject matter no longer exists, there is nothing to remove; the contract has already been fulfilled or terminated by the destruction.
The commentary analogizes to the impossibility of initiating iqaalah after total destruction.
3
Distinguish sold item from price
The condition applies only to the sold item, not the price (thaman). The price is not the subject of the contract in the same way; it is established by the contract and follows it. Hence, destruction of the price does not prevent iqaalah.
This asymmetry reinforces Hanafi reasoning: the sold item is the locus (mahl) of the contract, while the price is secondary.
4
Apply to total destruction before iqaalah
If the sold item perishes entirely before the parties agree to iqaalah, the iqaalah is invalid and the sale remains as is. If it perishes after iqaalah but before delivery to the seller, the iqaalah is invalidated and the sale returns.
Destruction after iqaalah but before delivery is treated like destruction before iqaalah in effect.
5
Apply to partial destruction
If only part of the sold item perishes, iqaalah is prevented only to the extent of the destroyed part. The rest of the item can still be subject to iqaalah with a proportional reduction in the price.
This is based on the principle that the part is considered with the whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وهلاك المبيع يمنع أي صحتها لما قدمنا أن من شرطها بقاء المبيع لأنها رفع . يقل له نعم فراجعه قول المصنف : (وهلاك المبيع يمنع قال الرملي :أقول : وكذا هلاكه بعد الإقالة وقبل التسليم يبطلها قال في البزازية: هلاك المبيع بعد الإقالة قبل التسليم مبطل وفي مجمع الفتاوى، ولو تقايلا ثم هلك المبيع قبل التسليم بطلت الإقالة وفي مجمع الرواية شرح القدوري قال في شرح الطحاوي: أو هلك المبيع بعد الإقالة قبل التسليم إلى البائع بطلت الإقالة، ومثله في كثير الكتب، ووجهه مأخوذ من قولهم لا تتم إلا بالقبض . قال في الخلاصة: ولو جاء المشتري إلى البائع وقال إنه قام علي بثمن غال فرد عليه البائع ما قبض من الثمن ولكن لم يقبض ما باع لا تتم الإقالة والشرط الإعطاء من الجانبين .اهـ. ولتمامها حكم إنشائها فكما لا يجوز إنشاؤها بعد هلاك المبيع فكذا هلاكه يبطلها. وقدم هذا الشارح في قوله هي فسخ أنه إذا تعذر جعلها فسخاً بأن ولدت المبيعة بعد القبض أو هلك المبيع فإنها تبطل ويبقى البيع على حاله والله تعالى أعلم اهـ. قلت : وما من العقد فكان حكماً محله. قيد بالمبيع لأن هلاك الثمن لا بمنعها لكونه ليس بمحل لكونه يثبت بالعقد وهو وهو يعقبه فلا يكون محلاً لأن المحل شرط وهو سابق فتنافيا، ولذا بطل البيع بهلاك المبيع قبل القبض دون الثمن قوله : (وهلاك بعضه بقدره) أي هلاك بعض المبيع يمنعها بقدر الهالك لأن الجزء معتبر بالكل. وفي بيع المقايضة إذا هلك أحدهما صحت، في الباقي . منهما وعلى المشتري قيمة الهالك إن كان ،قيمياً ومثله إذا كان مثلياً فيسلمه إلى صاحبه ويسترد العين إلا إذا هلكا بخلاف البدلين في الصرف إذا هلك لعدم التعيين ولذا لا يلزمهما إلا رد المثل بعدها. وفي السراج الوهاج : اشترى عبداً بنقرة فضة أو بمصوغ مما يتعين صحت وعلى هلك العبد في يد المشتري ثم تقايلا والفضة قائمة في يد البائع فتقابضا ثم البائع رد الفضة بعينها ويسترد من المشتري قيمة العبد وفي البزازية : تقايلا فأبق العبد من يد المشتري وعجز عن تسليمه تبطل الإقالة .اهـ. وأشار إلى أن البيع إذا هلك بعد الإقالة بطلت وعاد البيع. قيد بالهلاك لأنه لو باع صابوناً رطباً ثم تقايلا بعدما جف فنقص وزنه لا يجب على المشتري شيء لأن كل المبيع باق؛ كذا في فتح القدير وأشار بعدم اشتراط بقاء جميع المبيع على حاله إلى أنه لو اشترى أرضاً مع الزرع وحصده المشتري ثم تقايلا صحت في الأرض بحصتها من الثمن بخلاف ما إذا أدرك الزرع في يده ثم تقايلا فإنها لا تجوز لأن العقد إنما ورد على القصيل دون الحنطة ؛ كذا في القنية وإلى أن الاعتبار لما دخل في البيع متسوداً فلو ذكره عن الخلاصة قائماً مبني على غير الصحيح فقد ذكر المسألة في البزازية ثم قال : فمن قال البيع ينعقد بالتعاطي من أحد الجانبين جعله إقالة وهو الصحيح ومن شرط القبض من الجانبين لا يكون إقالة اهـ. قوله: (وهو محله) أي والمبيع محل العقد قوله : (قيد بالمبيع) كان نسخته ليس فيها التصريح بحكم الثمن وإلا فالذي رأيته في المتن وعليه كتب في النهر التصريح به قبل قوله «وهلاك المبيع يمنع حيث قال : وهلاك الثمن لا يمنع الإقالة قوله : (وفي بيع المقايضة الخ) بالياء المثناة التحتية بأن تبايعا عبداً بجارية فهلك العبد في يد بائع الجارية ثم أقالا البيع في الجارية وجب رد قيمة العبد، ولا تبطل بهلاك أحدهما بعد وجودهما لأن كل واحد منهما مبيع فكان المبيع وتمامه في العناية . قوله : (إلا إذا هلكا) أي فتبطل الإقالة وقوله بخلاف البدلين «الخ) أي فإن هلاكهما جميعاً غير مانع مع أن لكل واحد منهما حكم المبيع والثمن كما في المقايضة لأنهما لما لم يتعينا لم تتعلق الإقالة بأعيانهما لو كانا قائمين بل رد المقبوض ورد مثله سيان فصار هلاكهما كقيامهما، وفي المقايضة تعلقت بأعيانهما قائمين فمتى هلكا لم يبق شيء من المعقود عليه ترد الإقالة عليه؛ كذا في العناية . قوله : إذا هلك بعد الإقالة أي قبل التسليم إلى البائع كما قوله : (وإلى أن الاعتبار لما دخل في البيع مقصوداً) قال الرملي : يؤخذ منه جواب حادثة الفتوى : اشترى حماراً موكفاً وقبضه فهلك إكافه عنده ثم تقايلا لا يضمن وكذا إذا استهلكه، وإذا كان باقياً يرده لأنها من المبيع وإن دخلت تبعاً، ومثله الشجر إذا دخل تبعاً وهذا على غير الرقم الآخر، وأما على الرقم الآخر فكل شيء موجود مر.
His saying: (And destruction of the sold item prevents) meaning its validity, because we have presented that among its conditions is the existence of the sold item, because it is a removal. It is said to him... check his saying. The author said: (And destruction of the sold item prevents). Al-Ramli said: I say: Likewise, its destruction after the iqaalah and before delivery invalidates it. It said in al-Bazzaziyya: Destruction of the sold item after iqaalah before delivery is invalidating. And in Majma' al-Fatawa: If they did iqaalah and then the sold item perished before delivery, the iqaalah is invalid. And in Majma' al-Riwaya Sharh al-Quduri, it said in Sharh al-Tahawi: Or if the sold item perishes after iqaalah before delivery to the seller, the iqaalah is invalid. And similar in many books. Its reasoning is taken from their saying: It is not completed except by delivery. It said in al-Khulasa: If the buyer came to the seller and said that he incurred a high price, and the seller returned what he received of the price but did not receive what he sold, the iqaalah is not completed, and the condition is giving from both sides. End. And for its completion, the ruling of its inception: just as it is not permissible to initiate it after the destruction of the sold item, so too its destruction invalidates it. And this commentator presented in his saying 'it is a rescission' that if it becomes impossible to make it a rescission, such as if the sold female gives birth after delivery or the sold item perishes, then it is invalid and the sale remains as it was. And Allah knows best. End. I say: And what is from the contract becomes a ruling and its place. He restricted it to the sold item because destruction of the price does not prevent it, because it is not the place, because it is established by the contract and it follows it, so it is not a place because the place is a condition and it is prior, so they are contradictory. Therefore the sale is invalidated by destruction of the sold item before delivery but not the price. His saying: (And destruction of part of it according to its portion) meaning destruction of part of the sold item prevents it according to the amount destroyed, because the part is considered with the whole. And in a barter sale, if one of them perishes, it is valid in the remaining part. And on the buyer is the value of the perished item if it is a commodity of value, and similarly if it is a fungible, he delivers its like to his companion and takes back the object, unless both perish. Unlike the two considerations in currency exchange if they perish due to lack of specification, therefore they are only required to return the like after it. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: He bought a slave with a lump of silver or with something manufactured that is specified, it is valid. And if the slave perishes in the buyer's hand and then they do iqaalah, and the silver is present in the seller's hand, they take possession, then the seller returns the same silver and recovers from the buyer the value of the slave. And in al-Bazzaziyya: They did iqaalah and the slave ran away from the buyer's hand and he is unable to hand over it, the iqaalah is invalid. End. And he indicated that the sale, if it perishes after iqaalah, is invalidated and the sale returns. He restricted it to destruction because if he sold wet soap and then they did iqaalah after it dried and its weight decreased, nothing is owed by the buyer because all of the sold item remains; as in Fath al-Qadir. And he indicated that it is not required that all of the sold item remain in its state, that if he bought land with crop and the buyer harvested it and then they did iqaalah, it is valid in the land with its share of the price, unlike if the crop ripened in his hand and then they did iqaalah, then it is not permissible because the contract was only for the unripe grain, not the wheat; as in al-Qunyah. And that the consideration, since it entered the sale as intended, so if he mentioned from al-Khulasa 'existing' based on incorrect, he mentioned the issue in al-Bazzaziyya and said: So whoever says the sale is concluded by mutual dealing from one side makes it an iqaalah, and it is correct; and whoever requires delivery from both sides, it is not an iqaalah. End. His saying: (And it is its place) meaning the sold item is the place of the contract. His saying: (He restricted it to the sold item) — his copy did not contain explicit mention of the ruling of the price, otherwise what I saw in the text and upon which are written in al-Nahr is explicit before his saying 'And destruction of the sold item prevents' where he said: And destruction of the price does not prevent iqaalah. His saying: (And in barter sale etc.) with the underdotted ya, meaning they sold a slave for a slave-girl, and the slave perishes in the hand of the seller of the slave-girl, then they cancel the sale regarding the slave-girl, the value of the slave must be returned. And it is not invalidated by destruction of one of them after their existence because each one is a sold item, so it is the sold item, and its completion is in al-Inayah. His saying: (Unless both perish) meaning then the iqaalah is invalid. And his saying 'unlike the two considerations' etc. means that their destruction together is not a preventer, even though each one has the ruling of sold item and price as in barter, because since they are not specified, iqaalah is not attached to their specific individuals if they were existing; rather, returning what was received and returning its like are the same, so their destruction becomes like their existence. In barter, it is attached to their specific individuals when existing; so when they perish, nothing of the subject matter remains for iqaalah to be applied to. As in al-Inayah. His saying: When it perishes after iqaalah means before delivery to the seller, as his saying: (And that the consideration, since it entered the sale as intended) Al-Ramli said: From it is taken the answer to a fatwa incident: He bought a donkey with a pack-saddle and took possession, then the pack-saddle perished in his hand, then they did iqaalah — he is not liable, and similarly if he consumed it. And if it remains, he returns it because it is part of the sold item even if it entered as incidental. And similarly trees if they enter as incidental. And this is according to the other reading. As for the other reading, everything existing is mentioned.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the matn statement 'Destruction of the sold item prevents iqaalah' from Kanz al-Daqaiq. It establishes that the condition for a valid iqaalah is the existence of the sold item at the time of rescission. If the item is destroyed before iqaalah, iqaalah is impossible; if destroyed after iqaalah but before delivery, the iqaalah is invalidated and the original sale remains. The commentary explains that this ruling applies only to the sold item, not the price, because the price is not the subject matter of the contract. It then discusses partial destruction: if part of the sold item perishes, iqaalah is only prevented proportionally. In barter (muqayadah), each item is considered a sold item, so destruction of one does not prevent iqaalah on the other, but if both perish, iqaalah is invalid. The passage also addresses specific cases: runaway slave, wet soap that dries, land with crop, and a saddle that perishes. It includes a dispute about whether iqaalah can be concluded by unilateral action. Throughout, the commentary relies on earlier Hanafi authorities like al-Bazzaziyya, al-Khulasa, and al-Inayah.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqaalah): Rescission of a contract by mutual consent
هلاك (halak): Destruction or perishing of the sold item
مبيع (mabee'): The sold item, the subject matter of the sale
مقايضة (muqayadah): Barter sale where each party gives a commodity as both price and sold item

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the sold item perishes after the parties have agreed to iqaalah but before delivery to the seller?
A) The iqaalah is invalidated and the sale remains.
B) The iqaalah is valid and the buyer keeps the item.
C) The sale is automatically rescinded.
D) The seller must compensate the buyer.

Key Takeaway
Iqaalah requires the sold item to exist; destruction of the item before or after iqaalah (before delivery) invalidates the rescission, and the original sale remains binding.

6 min
Destruction of Sold Item Prevents Rescission
Commentary on the ruling that destruction of the sold item prevents rescission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on rescission when the sold item is destroyed, and explain the legal reasoning based on the concept of mahall (locus).

Core Idea
Destruction of the sold item prevents rescission; the price's destruction does not.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a key condition for the validity of rescission (iqalah) in a sale. The author explains that the sold item is the locus (mahall) of the contract, so its existence is fundamental. If the item is wholly or partially destroyed before rescission, the rescission is invalid or only partial proportionally.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the core ruling: if the sold item is destroyed before rescission, rescission is invalid. If only part is destroyed, rescission is invalid to the extent of the destroyed part.
The ruling applies to both full and partial destruction.
2
Understand the reasoning
The sold item is the locus (mahall) of the contract. The locus must exist for the contract to operate. The price is not the locus because it is established by the contract and follows it. Therefore, destruction of the price does not rescind the contract.
The concept of mahall is crucial in Hanafi contract theory.
3
Apply to partial destruction
If part of the sold item is destroyed, the prohibition of rescission applies proportionally—only the undestroyed part can be the subject of rescission with a corresponding reduction in price.
The part is treated like the whole in this regard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب الإقالة
العقد فكان حكماً محله. قيد بالمبيع لأن هلاك الثمن لا يمنعها لكونه ليس بمحل لكونه يثبت بالعقد وهو يعقبه فلا يكون محلاً لأن المحل شرط وهو سابق فتنافيا، ولذا بطل البيع بهلاك المبيع قبل القبض دون الثمن قوله : (وهلاك بعضه بقدره) أي هلاك بعض المبيع يمنعها بقدر الهالك لأن الجزء معتبر بالكل.
Book of Sales, Chapter of Rescission. The contract was a ruling its locus. It is restricted to the sold item because destruction of the price does not prevent it, since it is not the locus, because it is established by the contract and it follows it, so it cannot be a locus because the locus is a condition and it precedes, so they are contradictory. Therefore, the sale becomes void by the destruction of the sold item before delivery, but not by the price. He said: (And destruction of part of it proportionally) meaning destruction of part of the sold item prevents it to the extent of the destroyed part, because the part is considered like the whole.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the ruling that the existence of the sold item is a condition for the validity of rescission (إقالة). The author explains that the 'locus' of the contract is the sold item itself, not the price, because the price is established by the contract and follows it, while the locus must precede the contract. Hence, destruction of the sold item before delivery renders the sale void, but destruction of the price does not. Then the matn phrase 'وهلاك بعضه بقدره' is explained: if part of the sold item is destroyed, the rescission is prohibited proportionally to the destroyed part, since the part is treated like the whole in this regard.

Key Terms
الإقالة (al-iqālah): Rescission of a sale by mutual agreement of the parties.
المبيع (al-mabīʿ): The object of sale, the sold item.
محل (maḥall): The locus of the contract; the subject matter upon which the contract operates.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If half of the purchased slave is destroyed before rescission, what is the ruling?
A) Rescission is valid for the whole price.
B) Rescission is valid proportionally to the remaining part.
C) Rescission is invalid entirely.
D) Rescission is valid only if the buyer compensates for the destroyed part.

Key Takeaway
The existence of the sold item is a condition for valid rescission; if it is destroyed, rescission is void; partial destruction limits rescission proportionally.

Rescission in Barter Sale When One Item Perishes
Ruling on rescission with one item perished in barter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on rescission in a barter sale when one item perishes, distinguishing between qimi and mithli compensation and identifying the exception when both items perish.

Core Idea
In barter sale, if one item perishes, rescission remains valid for the other, with compensation based on the perished item's nature.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in barter sales (bay' al-muqayadah) where one of the exchanged items perishes before rescission. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling that preserves the validity of rescission while ensuring fair compensation. Understanding this rule prevents disputes and clarifies the limits of rescission in non-currency exchanges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is rescission (iqalah) in a barter sale. The ruling is that rescission is valid (sahih) for the remaining item even if one item has perished.
Rescission is not invalidated by the loss of one item; it continues for what remains.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
If only one item perishes, rescission is valid with compensation. If both perish, rescission is invalid (since nothing remains to return).
The exception is when both items are lost; then the contract cannot be reversed.
3
Apply the compensation rule
The party who received the perished item must compensate: pay its value (qimah) if it is a qimi (non-fungible), or deliver its like (mithl) if it is a mithli (fungible).
This distinction is based on the nature of the item: qimi items vary in quality; mithli items are standardized.
4
Contrast with currency exchange (sarf)
In sarf, the exchanged items (currency) are not specified, so if they perish, the obligation is only to return the equivalent (mithl) of what was received, not the specific coins.
The difference stems from specification (ta'yin): in barter, each item is a specific 'ayn; in sarf, the currency is not specified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي بيع المقايضة إذا هلك أحدهما صحت، في الباقي منهما وعلى المشتري قيمة الهالك إن كان قيمياً ومثله إذا كان مثلياً فيسلمه إلى صاحبه ويسترد العين إلا إذا هلكا بخلاف البدلين في الصرف إذا هلك لعدم التعيين ولذا لا يلزمهما إلا رد المثل بعدها.
And in a barter sale, if one of them perishes, the rescission is valid for the remaining of the two, and upon the buyer is the value of the perished item if it is a generic item of value (qimi), and its like (mithl) if it is a standard item (mithli); so he hands it over to its owner and takes back the specific item, except if both perish, contrary to the two exchange items in currency exchange (sarf) if they perish due to non-specification; hence they are only obliged to return the equivalent after it.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of rescission in a barter sale (مقايضة), where each party gives a specific item as both price and sold commodity. If one of the items perishes after the contract but before rescission, the rescission is still valid for the remaining item, but the party who received the perished item must compensate its value (if it is a qimi, i.e., an item varying in quality) or its like (if it is a mithli, i.e., a fungible). The exception is if both items perish, then rescission becomes invalid. This contrasts with currency exchange (صرف), where the items (currency) are not specified; if they perish, only the equivalent in value or kind is returned because the specific coins are not identified.

Key Terms
بيع المقايضة (bayʿ al-muqāyaḍah): A barter sale where goods are exchanged for goods, each being both price and sold item.
قيمي (qīmī): An item that is not fungible; its value is assessed by estimation.
مثلي (mithlī): A fungible item that can be replaced by another of the same kind and measure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a barter sale, if both items perish before rescission, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) Rescission is valid, and each party compensates the other.
B) Rescission is invalid because nothing remains to return.
C) Rescission is valid only if the items are mithli.
D) Rescission is valid only if both parties agree to waive compensation.

Key Takeaway
In a barter sale, rescission remains valid when one item perishes, with compensation based on the item's nature (qimi or mithli), but invalid if both perish.

Effect of Changes in the Sold Item on Rescission
Rulings on rescission when the sold item changes: drying, cutting crops, cutting trees, amputation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for rescission when a sold item has changed, distinguish between changes that invalidate rescission and those that do not, and apply the rule to scenarios like cut trees, harvested crops, and injured slaves.

Core Idea
Rescission remains valid if the item's essence survives; only a fundamental change (like stalks becoming grain) invalidates it.

Rescission (iqala) is the mutual annulment of a sale. The general rule is that the sold item must be in its original condition for rescission to be valid. However, the Hanafi school recognizes that items may undergo changes after the sale but before rescission. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explores borderline cases: wet soap that dries, crops that are harvested or ripen, trees that are cut, and a slave who loses a hand. The rulings reveal a consistent principle: as long as the item's identity is not fundamentally altered, rescission is valid, and compensation for changes is not automatically owed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Master Principle
Start by noting the opening phrase: 'قيّد بالهلاك'—the ruling is restricted to destruction. This establishes that rescission is valid unless the item is destroyed. Changes short of destruction do not necessarily block rescission.
Destruction (halak) means the item ceases to exist. Drying, cutting, amputation are not destruction.
2
Apply to Wet Soap Drying
Consider wet soap that dries and loses weight. The essence of the soap remains, so rescission is valid with no compensation. The key is that the item still exists as soap.
Weight loss does not change identity.
3
Apply to Land with Crops
Two subcases: (a) buyer harvests the crop (stalks still present) → rescission valid for land with its share of price, and the harvested crop belongs to buyer. (b) crop ripens into grain → rescission invalid because the contract was for stalks (qasil), not grain. The item's identity changed fundamentally.
Ripening transforms the subject matter of the sale.
4
Apply to Trees Cut on Land
Two opinions: (1) rescission valid, trees go to buyer if seller knew; if not, seller has option. (2) seller takes value of trees. The author leans toward the second to avoid seller's loss. This shows the ikhtilaf within the school.
The first opinion treats cutting like harvesting; the second treats trees as part of the sale that seller should be compensated for.
5
Apply to Slave with Amputated Hand
Similar to trees: if buyer took compensation (arsh) for the hand, rescission is still valid, and seller gets none of that arsh if he knew. If he did not know, he has option. Reason: arsh is not part of the sale price.
The hand itself is gone, but the slave remains; arsh is a separate claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيد بالهلاك لأنه لو باع صابوناً رطباً ثم تقايلا بعدما جف فنقص وزنه لا يجب على المشتري شيء لأن كل المبيع باق؛ كذا في فتح القدير وأشار بعدم اشتراط بقاء جميع المبيع على حاله إلى أنه لو اشترى أرضاً مع الزرع وحصده المشتري ثم تقايلا صحت في الأرض بحصتها من الثمن بخلاف ما إذا أدرك الزرع في يده ثم تقايلا فإنها لا تجوز لأن العقد إنما ورد على القصيل دون الحنطة ؛ كذا في القنية وإلى أن الاعتبار لما دخل في البيع متسوداً فلو اشترى أرضاً فيها أشجار فقطعها ثم تقايلا صحت الإقالة بجميع الثمن ولا شيء للبائع من قيمة الأشجار وتسلم الأشجار للمشتري هذا إذا علم البائع بقطعها، فإن لم يعلم به وقتها خير إن شاء أخذها بجميع الثمن وإن شاء ترك. وإن اشترى عبداً فقطعت يده وأخذ أرشها ثم تقايلا صحت الإقالة ولزمه بجميع الثمن ولا شيء للبائع من أرش اليد إذا علم وقت الإقالة أنه قطعت يده وأخذ أرشها، وإن لم يعلم يخير الأخذ بين بجميع الثمن وبين الترك؛ كذا في القنية. ورقم برقم آخر أن الأشجار لا تسلم للمشتري وللبائع أخذ قيمتها منه لأنها موجودة وقت البيع بخلاف الأرش فإنه لم يدخل في البيع أصلاً لا قصداً ولا ضمناً .اهـ.
It is restricted to destruction because if he sold wet soap and then they rescind after it dried and its weight decreased, nothing is due from the buyer because all of the sold item remains; likewise in Fath al-Qadir. And he indicated, by not requiring that all of the sold item remain in its original condition, that if he bought land with crops and the buyer harvested the crop then they rescind, the rescission is valid for the land with its share of the price, contrary to if the crop ripened in his hand then they rescind, then it is not permissible because the contract was only for the stalks, not the grain; likewise in al-Qunyah. And that consideration, since it entered the sale as intended, so if he bought land with trees and cut them then they rescind, the rescission is valid for the entire price, and nothing is due to the seller for the value of the trees, and the trees are handed over to the buyer, this if the seller knew of the cutting; if he did not know at the time, he has the option: if he wishes he takes it for the entire price, if he wishes he leaves. And if he bought a slave and his hand was cut off and he took compensation for it, then they rescind, the rescission is valid and he is liable for the entire price, and nothing is due to the seller for the compensation for the hand, if he knew at the time of rescission that his hand was cut off and he took compensation; if he did not know, he has the option between taking it for the entire price or leaving; likewise in al-Qunyah. And it is numbered with another number that the trees are not handed over to the buyer, and the seller takes their value from him, because they existed at the time of the sale, unlike the compensation, which did not enter the sale at all, neither intentionally nor implicitly.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage discusses various scenarios where the sold item has undergone changes but is not destroyed. The key principle is that if the item still exists (e.g., soap that has dried and lost weight), rescission is valid without compensation because the essence remains. For land with crops: if the buyer harvests the crops (which were part of the sale), rescission is valid for the land, and the crops belong to the buyer. But if the crops ripened and became grain, rescission is invalid because the contract was for the stalks, not the grain—so the item has changed fundamentally. For trees on land: if the buyer cuts them, two opinions exist. According to the first, rescission is valid for full price, and trees go to buyer if seller knew; if not, seller has option. According to the second opinion (رقم آخر), trees are not handed to buyer; seller can take their value, since trees existed at sale. For a slave with a cut hand: similar rules apply; the compensation (أرش) for the hand does not enter the sale, so seller gets nothing for it if he knew; otherwise option.

Key Terms
أرش (arsh): Compensation paid for injury to a slave, e.g., for a cut hand.
قصيل (qaṣīl): Stalks of grain before the grain forms; the subject of sale when crops are sold standing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, why is rescission invalid when the crop ripens into grain before rescission?
A) Because the buyer took possession and changed the item.
B) Because the contract was for stalks (qasil), not grain, so the item's identity changed.
C) Because the seller did not consent to the ripening.
D) Because the land is no longer usable for crops.

Key Takeaway
Rescission (iqala) remains valid if the sold item's essence persists; only a change that alters the very subject of the contract (like stalks to grain) invalidates it.

Rescission in Salam: Validity Despite Destruction of Subject Matter
Ruling on rescission in salam with talil and author's note

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on rescission in salam and explain why it is valid even if the subject matter is destroyed, applying the principle that a debt has the ruling of a specific item.

Core Idea
Rescission in salam is valid even if the muslam fih is destroyed — because it is a debt, not a tangible item.

In regular sales, rescission requires the sold item to still exist. But salam is a special forward sale. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains why rescission in salam is an exception: the subject matter is a debt (dayn) that has the ruling of a specific item (hukm al-ayn). We will walk through the ruling, its reason, and how it applies whether the capital is a specific item or a debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exception to the general condition
The general condition for rescission is that the sold item must exist. But salam is an exception because the subject matter is not a specific existing item but a debt.
The passage begins by stating 'there is no objection to the condition of the sold item's existence' – implying this condition is waived for salam.
2
Understand the legal nature of muslam fih
The muslam fih (subject matter of salam) is a debt (dayn), but it has the ruling of a specific item (hukm al-ayn) for certain rules, such as not being substituted before receipt.
This dual nature is key: it is a debt, so its physical existence is not required for rescission.
3
Apply the ruling to different types of capital
The capital (ra's al-mal) can be a specific item (ayn) or a debt (dayn). If it exists, it is returned; if destroyed, its like or value is returned. If it is a debt, it is returned by its like regardless of existence.
The text details each scenario: 'if it was destroyed, the like if fungible, value if non-fungible'.
4
Consider the post-receipt scenario
If rescission occurs after the seller has received the muslam fih, the buyer returns the specific item he received, because it is now specified.
This is consistent with the idea that post-receipt, the debt is no longer abstract.
5
Reflect on the author's additional note
The author adds that the seller takes the value of anything implicitly or explicitly included, but not what never entered the contract. This repels harm from the seller.
This is a separate principle about implicit inclusion, relevant to sales generally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أنه لا يرد على اشتراط قيام المبيع لصحة الإقالة إقالة السلم قبل قبض المسلم فيه لأنها صحيحة، سواء كان رأس المال عيناً أو ديناً، وسواء كان قائماً في يد المسلم إليه أو هالكاً لأن المسلم فيه وإن كان ديناً فحقيقة فله حكم العين حتى لا يجوز الاستبدال به قبل قبضه، وإذا صحت فإن كان رأس المال عيناً قائمة ردت، وإن كانت هالكة رد المثل إن كان مثلياً، والقيمة إن كان قيمياً، وإن كان ديناً رد قائماً أو هالكاً لعدم التعيين، وكذا إقالته بعد قبض المسلم إليه وإن كان قائماً ويرد رب السلم عين المقبوض لكونه متعيناً؛ كذا في البدائع والله سبحانه أعلم. مثله وقت البيع للبائع أخذ قيمته دخل ضمناً أو قصداً، وكل شيء لم يدخل أصلاً لا قصداً ولا ضمناً ليس للبائع أخذه، وأقول : ينبغي ترجيح هذا لما فيه من دفع الضرر عنه تأمل.
Then know that there is no objection to the condition of the sold item's existence for the validity of rescission regarding the rescission of salam before receipt of the subject matter (muslam fih), because it is valid, whether the capital (ra's al-mal) is a specific item or a debt, and whether it exists in the hand of the one receiving salam (muslam ilayh) or is destroyed, because the subject matter (muslam fih) although it is a debt, in reality it has the ruling of a specific item so that it is not permissible to substitute it before receipt. And if the rescission is valid, then if the capital was a specific existing item, it is returned; if it was destroyed, the like (mithl) is returned if it is a fungible, and the value (qimah) if it is a non-fungible; if it was a debt, it is returned whether existing or destroyed due to non-specification. Likewise, its rescission after the receipt of the muslam ilayh, even if it exists, and the owner of the salam (rabb al-salam) returns the specific item received because it is specified. This is mentioned in al-Bada'i'. And Allah knows best. Similarly, at the time of sale, the seller takes its value if it entered implicitly or intentionally, and anything that did not enter at all, neither intentionally nor implicitly, the seller does not take it. And I say: this should be preferred because it repels harm from him. Reflect.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage clarifies that the general condition that the sold item must exist for rescission does not apply to salam (salam) sales. In salam, the subject matter (المسلم فيه) is a debt (dayn) that is not yet received, but legally it is treated like a specific item (عين حكمي). Therefore, rescission is valid even if the subject matter is destroyed, because what is owed is an obligation to deliver. The capital (رأس المال) can be either a specific item or a debt; if it is a specific item and it still exists, it is returned; if destroyed, its equivalent or value is given. If the capital is a debt, it is returned by its like regardless of existence. After receipt, the rescission is similar. The author then adds his own note: the seller can take the value of something that was implicitly or explicitly included in the sale, but not things that never entered the contract at all. He prefers this view to prevent loss to the seller.

Key Terms
السلم (al-salam): A forward sale where payment is made in advance for delivery of a specified commodity later.
المسلم فيه (al-muslam fīh): The subject matter of salam; the commodity to be delivered later.
رأس المال (ra's al-māl): The capital advanced by the buyer in a salam contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Returned if existing, else its value.
B) Returned by its like regardless of existence.
C) Not returned because contract is void.
D) Only if both parties agree on a new price.

Key Takeaway
Rescission in salam is valid even if the subject matter is destroyed, because it is a debt (dayn) — the capital is returned according to its nature.

Effect of Increase on Rescission: Types and Timing
Refining the ruling on increase and rescission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules regarding how different types of increase (inseparable, separable, benefit-compensation) affect the validity of rescission (إقالة) and identify the key condition of timing (before or after delivery).

Core Idea
Inseparable increase never blocks rescission; separable increase blocks only after delivery.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq refines the ruling on how increase in the sold item impacts rescission. The author navigates multiple Hanafi sources to clarify that the type of increase and its timing are decisive. You will see how later Hanafi jurists restrict earlier absolute statements, and how the rule for benefit-compensation differs.

Guided Path
1
Identify Types of Increase
Classify the increase as inseparable (mutasila) like growth, separable (munfasila) like fruit or offspring, or benefit-compensation (badal al-manfa'a) like rent. Each has a different effect on rescission.
Inseparable cannot be removed without damage; separable can.
2
Recognize Timing Condition
Note that the timing of the increase (before or after delivery) is critical. The author restricts the rule to post-delivery increase.
Before delivery, any increase does not prevent rescission.
3
Synthesize Different Hanafi Opinions
Compare the statements from al-Khulasa, al-TatarKhaniyya, and al-Mujtaba. Al-Mujtaba is clearest: inseparable never prevents; separable prevents after delivery but not before.
The author resolves conflicts by restricting to post-delivery.
4
Apply Benefit-Compensation Exception
Understand that if the increase is badal al-manfa'a (e.g., rent), it never prevents rescission, and upon rescission the profit belongs to the buyer after oath.
This is unanimous among the Hanafi authorities cited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخلاصة: رجل باع من آخر كرماً فسلمه إليه فأكل المشتري نزله سنة ثم تقايلا لا يصح، وكذا إذا هلكت الزيادة المتصلة أو المنفصلة أو استهلكها الأجنبي اهـ. أقول : ينبغي تقييد المسألة بما إذا كانت هذه الزيادة حدثت بعد القبض لأنها لو كانت قبل القبض ينبغي أن لا تمنع الإقالة كما لا تمنع الرد بالعيب تأمل. وأقول : وإنما تمنع المنفصلة إذا كانت متولدة من المبيع، أما إذا لم تكن متولدة منه ككسب وغلة لا تمنع الفسخ بسائر أسباب الفسخ، وقد ذكر ذلك في الخامس والعشرين من جامع الفصولين فراجعه مع ما كتبناه عليه يظهر لك ذلك. وفي التتارخانية : وإن ازدادت الجارية ثم تقايلا فإن كان قبل القبض صحت الإقالة، سواء كانت الزيادة متصلة أو منفصلة، فهو صريح فيما تفقهناه والله تعالى هو الموفق. وفي المجتبى : الزيادة المتصلة لا تمنع الإقالة قبل القبض وبعده والمنفصلة تمنع بعده لا قبله اهـ. وفي التتارخانية من كتاب البيوع في الفصل الحادي عشر في الاختلاف الواقع بين البائع والمشتري بعد أن رمز للمحيط : وإن كانت الزيادة بدل المنفعة فإنهما يتحالفان بالإجماع فإذا تحالفا كان . الكسب للمشتري عندهم جميعاً كما لو حصل الفسخ بالرد بالعيب بعد القبض أو بإقالة بعد القبض فإنه يبقى الكسب للمشتري عندهم جميعاً .اهـ. قوله : (لأن المسلم إليه) كذا في النسخ والصواب «المسلم فيه» وكذا قوله الآتي «بعد قبض المسلم إليه»
And in al-Khulasa: A man sold a vineyard to another and handed it over, then the buyer ate its produce for a year, then they rescind, it is not valid. Similarly, if the inseparable increase (ziyada muttasila) or separable increase (munfasila) perishes, or if a third party consumes it. I say: It should be restricted to when this increase occurred after delivery, because if it was before delivery, it should not prevent rescission, just as it does not prevent return due to defect. Reflect. And I say: The separable increase only prevents if it is generated from the sold item; but if it is not generated from it, like profit and rent, it does not prevent annulment by other causes of annulment. This was mentioned in the twenty-fifth chapter of Jami' al-Fusulayn, so refer to it along with what we wrote on it, and it will become clear to you. And in al-TatarKhaniyya: If the slave-girl increased (in value/age) and then they rescind, if it is before delivery, the rescission is valid, whether the increase is inseparable or separable. This is explicit in what we have understood, and Allah Almighty is the grantor of success. And in al-Mujtaba: The inseparable increase does not prevent rescission before or after delivery, and the separable increase prevents after delivery but not before. In al-TatarKhaniyya from the Book of Sales in the eleventh chapter on the dispute between seller and buyer, after he referred to al-Muhit: If the increase is in lieu of benefit (badal al-manfa'a), then they take an oath by consensus, and if they take an oath, the profit belongs to the buyer according to all of them, just as if rescission occurs by return due to defect after delivery or by rescission after delivery, the profit remains with the buyer according to all of them. His statement: (because muslam ilayh) is in the copies, but the correct is 'muslam fih', and likewise his following statement 'after receipt of muslam ilayh'.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage extensively discusses the effect of increase (زيادة) in the sold item on the validity of rescission. The author starts by quoting al-Khulasa: if a buyer consumes the fruits of a vineyard for a year and then rescinds, the rescission is invalid. The author (the commentator) restricts this to cases where the increase occurred after delivery; before delivery, increase does not prevent rescission. He then distinguishes between increases that are generated from the sold item (متولدة) like fruit, and those that are not, like profit from trade or rent; the latter do not prevent rescission. He cites al-TatarKhaniyya that if a slave-girl increases (e.g., grows up), rescission before delivery is valid regardless of whether the increase is inseparable (like growth) or separable (like giving birth). Al-Mujtaba adds that inseparable increase never prevents rescission; separable increase prevents after delivery but not before. Finally, if the increase is in lieu of benefit (like rent), upon rescission the parties take oaths, and the profit belongs to the buyer unanimously. The passage concludes with a correction of a textual error: 'المسلم إليه' should be 'المسلم فيه'.

Key Terms
الزيادة المتصلة (al-ziyādat al-muttaṣilah): Inseparable increase in the sold item, e.g., growth in age or weight of a slave, which cannot be separated without damage.
الزيادة المنفصلة (al-ziyādat al-munfaṣilah): Separable increase, e.g., fruit, offspring, that can be separated from the sold item.
بدل المنفعة (badal al-manfaʿah): Compensation for benefit, e.g., rent from property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the refined Hanafi position in this passage, which type of increase never prevents rescission, even after delivery?
A) Seperable increase generated from the sold item
B) Inseparable increase (e.g., growth)
C) Benefit-compensation (e.g., rent)
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The validity of rescission after an increase depends on the type of increase and timing: inseparable and benefit-compensation never block, while separable blocks only if it occurs after delivery.

Cancellation after Altering Trees or Slave's Injury
Ruling on validity of cancellation when buyer altered the item before rescission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of iqālah when the buyer has altered the item and explain the seller's options based on his knowledge of the alteration.

Core Idea
Cancellation (iqālah) remains valid even after buyer alters the item; seller's knowledge at the time of rescission determines his right to choose.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in sales: what happens if the buyer alters the purchased item (e.g., cuts trees or accepts compensation for a slave's injury) and then the parties agree to cancel the sale? The Hanafi school holds that the cancellation is valid, but the seller's knowledge of the alteration affects whether he can demand compensation or rescind the cancellation. We will unpack two examples and a transmission difference within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage gives two examples: land with trees that the buyer cuts, and a slave whose hand is cut and the buyer accepts compensation (arsh). In both, the buyer alters the item before cancellation.
The ruling depends on whether the seller knew of the alteration at the time of iqālah.
2
State the basic ruling when seller knows
If the seller knows of the alteration at the time of rescission, the iqālah is valid with full price, but the seller has no claim for the value of the trees or the compensation. The buyer keeps the altered item.
This is the first transmission reported from al-Qunyah.
3
Explain the seller's choice when ignorant
If the seller did not know of the alteration at the time of iqālah, he has the option: either confirm the cancellation for the full price, or annul the cancellation (i.e., refuse the rescission).
This protects the seller from being forced into a deal he would not have agreed to had he known the item was altered.
4
Compare the second transmission on trees
Another transmission (رقم برقم آخر) states that for the trees, the seller may take their value from the buyer, because the trees existed at the time of sale and were part of the contract. This contrasts with the compensation for the slave's hand, which never entered the sale.
The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) prefers this second view as it prevents harm to the seller.
5
Trace the commentator's tarjih
The commentator says 'ينبغي ترجيح هذا' (it is appropriate to prefer this), referring to the second transmission that allows the seller to claim the value of the trees. This preference is based on the principle of preventing harm (دفع الضرر).
This shows how later Hanafi masters weigh conflicting transmissions to arrive at the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشترى أرضاً فيها أشجار فقطعها ثم تقايلا صحت الإقالة بجميع الثمن ولا شيء للبائع من قيمة الأشجار وتسلم الأشجار للمشتري هذا إذا علم البائع بقطعها، فإن لم يعلم به وقتها خير إن شاء أخذها بجميع الثمن وإن شاء ترك. وإن اشترى عبداً فقطعت يده وأخذ أرشها ثم تقايلا صحت الإقالة ولزمه بجميع الثمن ولا شيء للبائع من أرش اليد إذا علم وقت الإقالة أنه قطعت يده وأخذ أرشها، وإن لم يعلم يخير الأخذ بين بجميع الثمن وبين الترك؛ كذا في القنية. ورقم برقم آخر أن الأشجار لا تسلم للمشتري وللبائع أخذ قيمتها منه لأنها موجودة وقت البيع بخلاف الأرش فإنه لم يدخل في البيع أصلاً لا قصداً ولا ضمناً .اهـ. مثله وقت البيع للبائع أخذ قيمته دخل ضمناً أو قصداً، وكل شيء لم يدخل أصلاً لا قصداً ولا ضمناً ليس للبائع أخذه، وأقول : ينبغي ترجيح هذا لما فيه من دفع الضرر عنه تأمل.
If someone bought land with trees and cut them, then they rescinded the sale, the rescission is valid with the full price, and nothing is owed to the seller for the value of the trees, and the trees are delivered to the buyer. This is if the seller knew of the cutting. If he did not know at the time of rescission, he has the choice: if he wishes, he takes the land for the full price, and if he wishes, he leaves it. And if someone bought a slave whose hand was cut off and he took compensation for the hand, then they rescinded, the rescission is valid and the full price is binding on him, and nothing is owed to the seller for the compensation for the hand, if he knew at the time of rescission that his hand was cut and he took compensation. If he did not know, he has the choice between taking for the full price or leaving. So in al-Qunyah. And it is recorded in another transmission that the trees are not delivered to the buyer, and the seller may take their value from him, because they existed at the time of sale, unlike the compensation, for it did not enter the sale at all, neither intentionally nor implicitly. So similar: at the time of sale, if the seller takes their value, it entered implicitly or intentionally, and anything that did not enter at all neither intentionally nor implicitly, the seller may not take it. And I say: it is appropriate to prefer this because it prevents harm to him. Reflect.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the validity of cancellation (إقالة) when the buyer has altered the purchased item before the rescission. The author gives two examples: land with trees that the buyer cuts, and a slave whose hand is cut off and the buyer accepted compensation. In both cases, if the seller knew of the alteration at the time of rescission, the cancellation is valid and the buyer owes the full price but does not compensate the seller for the destroyed property (trees or hand compensation). If the seller did not know, he has the option to either complete the cancellation for the full price or annul the cancellation. The text then records a second transmission (رقم برقم آخر) stating that for the trees, the seller may take their value from the buyer because the trees were part of the sale, unlike the compensation which never entered the sale. The commentator prefers this latter view as it prevents harm to the seller. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that rescission is valid even if the item is altered, but the seller's knowledge affects his options.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqālah): Mutual rescission of a sale contract by both parties, cancelling the sale and returning the price and item.
أرش (arsh): Compensation paid for a defect or injury to a slave or property, e.g., for a cut hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The cancellation is valid, and the buyer keeps the trees for free without compensating the seller.
D) The cancellation is valid only if the seller receives the value of the trees.

Key Takeaway
Iqālah is valid even after the buyer alters the item, but the seller's knowledge at the time of rescission affects his options, and later Hanafi preference may grant him compensation for pre-sale property.

Cancellation of Salam Before Delivery: Valid Despite Non-Existence
Ruling on salam cancellation before delivery with proof from Hanafi sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cancellation of a salam contract before delivery and explain the legal reasoning that treats the salam item as a specific item for this purpose.

Core Idea
Salam cancellation before possession is valid because the salam item, though a debt, carries the ruling of a specific item (ayn).

This lesson examines a key exception to the general condition that the item must exist for cancellation. In salam (forward sale), the item is not present at contract, yet cancellation before delivery is valid. The Hanafi school treats the salam item as having the legal status of a specific item (ayn), enabling cancellation. We will explore the rules for returning the capital, whether it is a specific item, a fungible, or a debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general condition for cancellation validity
The general rule is that the item must exist (qiyam al-mabi') for cancellation to be valid. Identify the exception: cancellation of salam before delivery of the salam item is valid even though the item does not exist at the time of cancellation.
This exception is based on the legal fiction that the salam item, though a debt, is treated as a specific item.
2
Understand the nature of salam and its treatment
Salam is a sale of future goods; the price is paid upfront. The salam item is a debt (dayn) in the seller's obligation. The Hanafi school gives it the ruling of a specific item (hukm al-ayn) for certain purposes, including cancellation, so that it can be cancelled before delivery.
This treatment is not absolute; for example, the seller cannot substitute it without the buyer's consent.
3
Determine how the capital is returned upon cancellation
Upon valid cancellation, the capital (ra's al-mal) is returned according to its nature. If the capital is a specific item (ayn) and still exists, it is returned as is. If destroyed, the like (mithl) is returned if it is fungible (mithli), or its value (qimah) if non-fungible (qimi). If the capital is a debt, it is returned as it was, whether existing or destroyed.
This classification follows standard Hanafi rules for returning goods.
4
Confirm the ruling after possession
Cancellation after the seller has taken possession of the salam item is also valid, and the buyer returns the received item because it has become specified (mut'aayin).
This is consistent with the treatment of the salam item as a specific item after possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أنه لا يرد على اشتراط قيام المبيع لصحة الإقالة إقالة السلم قبل قبض المسلم فيه لأنها صحيحة، سواء كان رأس المال عيناً أو ديناً، وسواء كان قائماً في يد المسلم إليه أو هالكاً لأن المسلم إليه وإن كان ديناً فحقيقة فله حكم العين حتى لا يجوز الاستبدال به قبل قبضه، وإذا صحت فإن كان رأس المال عيناً قائمة ردت، وإن كانت هالكة رد المثل إن كان مثلياً، والقيمة إن كان قيمياً، وإن كان ديناً . رد قائماً أو هالكاً لعدم التعيين، وكذا إقالته بعد قبض المسلم إليه وإن كان قائماً ويرد رب السلم عين المقبوض لكونه متعيناً ؛ كذا في البدائع والله سبحانه أعلم. وفي الخلاصة: رجل باع من آخر كرماً فسلمه إليه فأكل المشتري نزله سنة ثم تقايلا لا يصح، وكذا إذا هلكت الزيادة المتصلة أو المنفصلة أو استهلكها الأجنبي اهـ. أقول : ينبغي تقييد المسألة بما إذا كانت هذه الزيادة حدثت بعد القبض لأنها لو كانت قبل القبض ينبغي أن لا تمنع الإقالة كما لا تمنع الرد بالعيب تأمل. وأقول : وإنما تمنع المنفصلة إذا كانت متولدة من المبيع، أما إذا لم تكن متولدة منه ككسب وغلة لا تمنع الفسخ بسائر أسباب الفسخ، وقد ذكر ذلك في الخامس والعشرين من جامع الفصولين فراجعه مع ما كتبناه عليه يظهر لك ذلك. وفي التتارخانية : وإن ازدادت الجارية ثم تقايلا فإن كان قبل القبض صحت الإقالة، سواء كانت الزيادة متصلة أو منفصلة، فهو صريح فيما تفقهناه والله تعالى هو الموفق. وفي المجتبى : الزيادة المتصلة لا تمنع الإقالة قبل القبض وبعده والمنفصلة تمنع بعده لا قبله اهـ. وفي التتارخانية من كتاب البيوع في الفصل الحادي عشر في الاختلاف الواقع بين البائع والمشتري بعد أن رمز للمحيط : وإن كانت الزيادة بدل المنفعة فإنهما يتحالفان بالإجماع فإذا تحالفا كان . الكسب للمشتري عندهم جميعاً كما لو حصل الفسخ بالرد بالعيب بعد القبض أو بإقالة بعد القبض فإنه يبقى الكسب للمشتري عندهم جميعاً .اهـ.
Then know that the condition of the existence of the item for the validity of cancellation is not contradicted by the cancellation of salam before taking possession of the salam item, because it is valid, whether the capital is a specific item or a debt, and whether it is existing in the hands of the seller or destroyed. Because the salam item, even though it is a debt in reality, has the ruling of a specific item, so replacement before possession is not allowed. If the cancellation is valid, if the capital is an existing specific item, it is returned; if it is destroyed, the like is returned if it is fungible, or its value if it is non-fungible; if it is a debt, it is returned whether existing or destroyed because it is not specified. Similarly, its cancellation after possession of the salam item, even if existing, and the buyer of salam returns the specific item received because it has become specified. So in al-Bada'i', and Allah knows best. And in al-Khulasah: A man sold a vineyard to another and delivered it, then the buyer ate its produce for a year, then they rescinded: it is not valid. Similarly, if the attached increase is destroyed, or the detached increase, or a third party consumed it. End. I say: It should be restricted to when this increase occurred after possession, because if it was before possession, it should not prevent cancellation, just as it does not prevent return due to defect. Reflect. And I say: The detached increase only prevents if it is generated from the item; if it is not generated from it, like earnings and rent, it does not prevent annulment by other causes of annulment. This is mentioned in the twenty-fifth of Jami' al-Fusulayn, so refer to it along with what we wrote on it, and it will become clear to you. And in al-Tatarkhaniyah: If the slave-girl increased in value and then the parties rescinded, then if it was before possession, the cancellation is valid, whether the increase is attached or detached. This is clear in what we have understood, and Allah is the giver of success. And in al-Mujtaba: Attached increase does not prevent cancellation before or after possession, and detached increase prevents after possession, not before. End. And in al-Tatarkhaniyah from the book of sales in the eleventh chapter on disputes between seller and buyer, after referring to al-Muhit: If the increase is in place of usufruct, they take an oath by consensus, and if they take an oath, the earnings belong to the buyer in their view, just as if annulment occurs by return due to defect after possession or by cancellation after possession, the earnings remain for the buyer in their view. End.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage addresses two conditions related to cancellation. First, the author explains that cancellation of a salam contract before delivery of the salam item is valid, despite the general condition that the item must exist for cancellation. This is because the salam item, though a debt, is treated like a specific item in legal rulings. The capital (price) is returned according to its nature: existing specific item, its like if fungible, or value if non-fungible; if a debt, it is returned as is. The second part discusses how increase in the item affects cancellation. Quoting al-Khulasah, cancellation of a vineyard is invalid if the buyer consumed its produce for a year, because the produce is a detached increase that occurred after possession. The commentator restricts this to increases after possession; increases before possession do not prevent cancellation, analogous to return due to defect. Furthermore, detached increase only prevents if it is generated from the item itself (e.g., fruit from a tree), not if it is separate earnings like wages. The passage then cites al-Tatarkhaniyah and al-Mujtaba to clarify that attached increase never prevents cancellation, while detached does after possession but not before. Finally, if the increase is in place of usufruct (e.g., rental income), mutual oaths are taken and earnings go to the buyer per consensus.

Key Terms
سلم (salam): A forward sale where the price is paid in advance for goods to be delivered later.
مثلي (mithli): Fungible goods that can be replaced by the same quantity and quality.
قيمي (qimi): Non-fungible goods that cannot be replaced exactly; value is used for compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on cancellation of a salam contract before receipt of the salam goods?
A) It is invalid because the goods do not exist.
B) It is valid, and the capital is returned as per its nature.
C) It is valid only if the capital is an existing specific item.
D) It is invalid because salam is a binding contract.

Key Takeaway
Salam cancellation before delivery is valid because the salam item, though a debt, is legally treated as a specific item, and the capital is returned according to its category (ayn, mithl, qimah, dayn).

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Effect of Increase on Cancellation: Attached vs Detached
Discussion of the effect of increase on cancellation, distinguishing attached/detached and timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the effect of attached and detached increase on the validity of cancellation (iqalah) and apply the Hanafi rule that timing and nature of the increase determine the outcome.

Core Idea
Attached increase never prevents cancellation; detached increase prevents cancellation only if it occurred after possession.

This lesson addresses a practical problem: when an item has increased in value or produced additional items (e.g., fruits, offspring, or fattening), does that affect the validity of cancellation? The Hanafi school distinguishes between increases that are physically attached (e.g., fattening) and those that are detached (e.g., picked fruit). It also considers whether the increase occurred before or after the buyer took possession. We will examine the rulings from multiple Hanafi sources as synthesized by Ibn Nujaym.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general rule: increase does not prevent cancellation if it occurred before possession
The commentator argues by analogy to return due to defect: increase before possession does not prevent cancellation. This is because the cancellation returns the item to the seller as it was before the increase was generated under the buyer's ownership.
This is a qiyas (analogy) with the well-established rule in return due to defect.
2
Distinguish attached vs detached increase after possession
After possession, attached increase (e.g., the animal fattened) does not prevent cancellation because it cannot be separated. Detached increase (e.g., picked fruit) prevents cancellation because it is a separate property that would have to be returned, and its return is complicated by consumer or destruction.
The passage from al-Mujtaba explicitly states: attached increase does not prevent before or after; detached increase prevents after, not before.
3
Apply the exception: detached increase that is not generated from the item does not prevent cancellation
The commentator refines the rule: only detached increases that are 'generated from the item' (e.g., fruit from tree, offspring) prevent cancellation. If the increase is separate earnings like rent or wages from the item, it does not prevent cancellation.
This is a crucial limitation: the increase must be a natural product of the item, not unrelated income.
4
Confirm the treatment of attached increase as never preventing
From al-Tatarkhaniyah and al-Mujtaba, attached increase never prevents cancellation, whether before or after possession. This is the relied-upon position.
The passage uses the example of a slave-girl increasing in value (attached increase like fattening) and then cancellation: before possession it is valid; after possession, the increase is inseparable so cancellation is still valid.

Key Terms
زيادة متصلة (ziyādah muttaṣilah): An increase that is physically attached to the item, e.g., fattening or growth.
زيادة منفصلة (ziyādah munfaṣilah): An increase that is detached from the item, e.g., fruits that have been picked, or offspring that have grown.
متولد (mutawallid): Generated from the item, e.g., fruit from a tree, offspring from an animal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following increases does NOT prevent cancellation (iqalah) after the buyer has taken possession?
A) Fruits of a tree that were picked after possession.
B) Offspring of an animal born after possession.
C) Fattening of an animal after possession.
D) Rent collected from the property after possession.

Key Takeaway
Attached increase never prevents cancellation; detached increase prevents only if it occurred after possession and is generated from the item itself.

Definition and Conditions of Murabahah and Tawliyah
Defining Murabahah and Tawliyah and explaining their conditions and permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of Murabahah and Tawliyah, identify their essential condition, and evaluate the rebuttal to al-Zayla'i's objection regarding usurped property.

Core Idea
Murabahah and Tawliyah require the original price to be fungible (mithli), ensuring trust-based sale permissibility.

In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq defines two important trust-based sales: Tawliyah (sale at cost) and Murabahah (sale at cost plus profit). These transactions are built on disclosure and honesty, and the Hanafi school sets a key condition: the original price must be a fungible commodity (mithli) to avoid ambiguity. We will explore the legal definitions, the rationale for permissibility, and a critical objection raised by al-Zayla'i regarding usurped property, which the author refutes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Definitions
Identify how the author defines Tawliyah as a sale at a previous price and Murabahah as a sale with that price plus profit. Note the linguistic roots and legal meanings.
The definitions are derived from the chapter heading and the author's explanation.
2
Identify the Essential Condition
Locate the condition that the first price must be mithli (fungible). Understand why this is required for clarity and trust.
Mithli refers to goods that can be replaced by weight, measure, or count without significant variation.
3
Analyze the Rationale for Permissibility
Recognize the public need for these sales, especially for those inexperienced in trade. The transaction is built on trust and avoids deception.
The hadith of the Prophet’s purchase from Abu Bakr illustrates the principle of paying a price rather than accepting a gift.
4
Examine the Objection and Rebuttal
Understand al-Zayla'i's objection regarding usurped property and how the author responds. Note that the same issue applies to both al-Hidayah and Kanz al-Daqaiq.
The objection is considered weak because the ruling for usurped property is based on analogy, not a flaw in the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المرابحة والتولية هي بيع بثمن سابق والمرابحة به وبزيادة وشرطهما كون الثمن الأول مثلياً وله أن يضم باب المرابحة والتولية شروع فيما يتعلق بالثمن من المرابحة والتولية والربا والصرف والبيع بالنسيئة بعد بيان أحكام المبيع وقدم المبيع لأصالته وكذا في البناية. وقدمتا أن أنواعه بالنسبة إلى الثمن أربعة هما والمساومة لا إلتفات فيها إلى الثمن الأول والرابع الوضيعة بأنقص من الأول، ولم يذكرهما لظهورهما، وهما جائزان لاستجماع شرائط الجواز والحاجة ماسة إلى هذا النوع من البيع لأن الغبي الذي لا يهتدي إلى التجارة يحتاج إلى أن يعتمد فعل الذكي المهتدي ويطيب نفسه بمثل ما اشترى وبزيادة ربح فوجب القول بجوازهما، ولذا كان مبناهما على الأمانة والاحتراز عن شبهة ،الخيانة وقد صح أن النبي الله لما أراد الهجرة ابتاع من أبي بكر رضي الله عنه بعيرين فقال النبي : «ولني أحدهما . فقال : هو لك بغير شيء. فقال: أما بغير ثمن فلا . قال السهيلي : سئل بعض العلماء لم لم يقبلها إلا بالثمن وقد أنفق عليه أبو بكر أضعاف ذلك وقد دفع إليه حين بنى بعائشة ثنتي عشرة أوقية حين قال له أبو بكر ألا تبني بأهلك فقال : لولا الصداق فدفع إليه ثنتي عشرة أوقية وشيئاً وهو عشرون درهماً. فقال : لتكون هجرته بنفسه وماله رغبة منه في استكمال فضلها إلى الله، وأن تكون على أتم الأحوال. والمرابحة في اللغة كما في الصحاح يقال بعته المتاع واشتريته منه مرابحة إذا سميت لكل قدر من الثمن ربحاً .اهـ. وأما التولية في اللغة فقال الشارحون : إنها مصدر ولي غيره إذا جعله والياً. وفي القاموس التولية في البيع نقل ما ملكه بالعقد الأول وبالثمن الأول من غير زيادة. وأما شرعاً فقال . (هي) أي التولية (بيع بثمن سابق والمرابحة به وبزيادة وأورد عليه الغصب وهو ما إذا ضاع المغصوب عند الغاصب وضمنه قيمته ثم وجده جاز له بيعه مرابحة وتولية على ما ضمن، وقد غفل الشارح الزيلعي فأورده على عبارة الهداية وهي نقل ما ملكه بالعقد الأول بالثمن الأول مع ربح أو لا، وادعى أن عبارة المؤلف أحسن وليس كما زعم لأن مسألة الغصب كما ترد على الهداية باعتبار أنه لا عقد فيها كذلك ترد على الكنز باعتبار أنه لا ثمن
Chapter of Murabahah and Tawliyah. It (Tawliyah) is a sale with a previous price, and Murabahah is a sale with that price plus a profit. Their condition is that the first price be of a fungible category (mithli). And he may include... The chapter of Murabahah and Tawliyah begins on what relates to the price from Murabahah, Tawliyah, Riba, Sarf, and credit sale after the explanation of rulings on the sold item, and the sold item was given precedence due to its fundamentality, likewise in al-Binayah. And they mentioned that its types with respect to price are four: Murabahah and Tawliyah are two, and Musawamah is where no attention is paid to the first price, and the fourth is Wadi'ah (discount) which is less than the first. He did not mention them because they are obvious. And they (Murabahah and Tawliyah) are permissible because they fulfill the conditions of permissibility, and there is a pressing need for this type of sale because the naïve person who cannot conduct trade needs to rely on the action of the intelligent expert, and his heart is satisfied with like what was bought plus profit, so it is necessary to rule on their permissibility. Therefore, they are based on trust and avoidance of the semblance of betrayal. It is authentically reported that when the Prophet wanted to migrate, he bought two camels from Abu Bakr (may Allah be pleased with him) and said, 'Give me the option of one of them.' Abu Bakr said, 'It is for you without anything.' He said, 'No, without a price is not acceptable.' Al-Suhayli said: Some scholars were asked why he did not accept it except with a price, whereas Abu Bakr had spent many times that on him, and he had given him when he married Ayesha twelve uqiyahs... [lengthy story]. And Murabahah in language, as in al-Sihah: you say I sold the goods to him and bought from him by Murabahah when you specify a profit for each amount of the price. For Tawliyah in language, the commentators said it is the verbal noun of 'wallā ghayrahu' when he makes him a deputy. In al-Qamus, Tawliyah in sale is transferring what he owns by the first contract and for the first price without increase. As for its legal definition, he said: (It) i.e. Tawliyah (is a sale with a previous price, and Murabahah is with it plus profit). An objection was raised regarding usurped property: if the usurped property is lost with the usurper and he guarantees its value, then he finds it, it is permissible for him to sell it by Murabahah and Tawliyah on the basis of what he guaranteed. Al-Zayla'i overlooked this and objected to the wording of al-Hidayah, claiming that the author's wording (of Kanz) is better. It is not as he claimed, because the issue of usurpation as it applies to al-Hidayah in that there is no contract, so it applies to al-Kanz in that there is no price.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on Murabahah and Tawliyah, two types of sales based on a previous purchase price. The author defines Tawliyah as a sale at the original price and Murabahah as a sale with a stated profit on that price. Their condition is that the first price must be of a fungible category (مثلي) to ensure clarity. The commentator explains that these sales are necessary for those inexperienced in trade, as they rely on the expertise of a trustworthy seller. The transaction is built on trust and avoidance of deception, as illustrated by the hadith of the Prophet's purchase of a camel from Abu Bakr. The language definitions are given: Murabahah is specifying a profit proportion; Tawliyah is transferring ownership at the same price. Legally, they are defined as sale with a previous price with or without profit. An objection is raised regarding a case of usurped property: if a usurper compensates for the lost item and later finds it, he may sell it at the guaranteed value via Murabahah or Tawliyah. The commentator notes that al-Zayla'i mistakenly criticized al-Hidayah on this point, but the same issue applies to al-Kanz because in usurpation there is no previous price or contract, yet the ruling is given by analogy.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murābaḥah): A sale where the seller discloses the original cost and adds a specified profit.
تولية (tawliyah): A sale where the seller transfers ownership at exactly the original purchase price, without profit or loss.
مثلي (mithlī): Fungible goods that can be replaced by weight, measure, or count without significant variation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for the original price in Murabahah and Tawliyah according to this passage?
A) It must be in gold or silver.
B) It must be a specific identifiable item.
C) It must be of a fungible category (mithli).
D) It must be the same as the current market price.

Key Takeaway
Murabahah and Tawliyah are permissible trust-based sales, provided the original price is a fungible (mithli) commodity to avoid ambiguity.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Definition of Murabahah and Tawliyah and Their Conditions
Defines murabahah and tawliyah and discusses conditions and objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define murabahah and tawliyah, state the condition that the first price must be mithli, and explain the objections to the standard definitions and how the author resolves them.

Core Idea
Murabahah and tawliyah are valid trust-based sales where the first price must be of a mithli type; their permissibility is supported by the Prophet's example.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides the classical definitions of murabahah (sale with profit) and tawliyah (sale at cost) based on a previous price. It emphasizes the key condition that the first price must be of a type that has an exact like (mithli). The commentary then raises several objections to these definitions and resolves them, culminating in the author's own comprehensive definitions that avoid all pitfalls.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the basic definitions
Read the opening line: 'هي بيع بثمن سابق والمرابحة به وبزيادة'. Identify that tawliyah is a sale at a previous price without profit, and murabahah is with an increase (profit).
Distinguish clearly between the two; tawliyah is literally 'transfer of ownership' at cost.
2
Identify the condition
The text states 'وشرطهما كون الثمن الأول مثلياً'. Understand that mithli means a price that has a like in kind and quantity (e.g., dirhams, dinars). This is essential because the price must be measureable and replaceable.
If the first price is qimi (unique, no like), these sales cannot be based on that price.
3
Follow the rationale for permissibility
The commentary explains that these sales fulfill conditions of validity and meet a public need for unsophisticated buyers. The hadith of the Prophet's purchase from Abu Bakr illustrates the principle of paying a price rather than accepting a gift, supporting the concept of murabahah.
Note the trust-based nature: 'مبناهما على الأمانة'.
4
Examine the objections
The author lists several cases that appear to challenge the standard definitions: (1) usurped property (ghasp), (2) property acquired by gift, inheritance, or bequest, (3) buying dirhams with dinars, (4) ambiguity in the term 'previous price' (whether 'ayn or like). For each, the text explains why the standard definition fails or requires clarification.
These objections are critiques of the definition's scope, not of permissibility.
5
See the author's refined definitions
The author proposes new definitions that explicitly exclude problematic cases. For tawliyah: 'نقل ما ملكه بغير عقد الصلح والهبة بشرط عوض...' etc. Understand how the new definition handles the objections by specifying the mode of acquisition and the nature of the price (mithli or value).
The author's definition is more precise; it serves as a template for legal drafting.
